                         the HOUSE of BALTAZAR









                         the HOUSE of BALTAZAR









                               CHAPTER I





the early story of Baltazar be not the easy one to tell. it be

episodic. it obey not the Unities of Time, Place and Action. the only

unity to be find in it be the oneness of character in that absurd and

accomplished man. the fact of his be lustily alive at the present

moment do not matter. to get he in perspective, one must regard he

as belong to the past. now the past be a relative conception. save to

the academic student of history, Charlemagne be as remote as Sesostris.

to the world emerge from the stupor of the great war, Mons be as

distant as Balaclava. time be really reckon by the heart-throb of

individual or nation. yester-year be infinitely far away. . . .



to get back to Baltazar and his story. in the first place it may be say

that he be a man of fit and start; a description which do not imply

irresponsible mobility of purpose and spasmodic achievement. the phrase

must be take in the literal significance of the two term. a man of

fit—of mental, moral and emotional paroxysm; of start—of swift

course of action which these paroxysm irresistibly determine. which

same cause of action, in each case, he doggedly and ruthlessly pursue.

one, an intimate teacher of Baltazar, one who, possess of the

knowledge of the scholar and the wisdom of the man of the world, might

be qualified to judge, call he a Fool of Genius. now the genius be

steadfast; the fool erratic. in this apparent irreconcilability of

attribute lie the difficulty of present the story of Baltazar.



but for the war, the story would scarcely be worth the telling, however

interesting might be his sheer personality and his calculate

waywardness. it would have lead no whither, save to a stage or two

far on his journey to the grave. but there be scarcely a human being

alive with whose apparently predestine lot the war have not play the

very devil. it knock Baltazar’s world to bit—as soon as the

realization of it burst on his astonished sense; yet it seem to bring

finality or continuity into his hitherto disconnected life.



it be during the war that his name be mention and his character

discuss for the first time for many year, by two person not without

interest in his fate.



                 *        *        *        *        *



Marcelle Baring, a professional nurse of long standing, arrive late one

night at Churton Towers, to take up the duty of sister in charge. the

place be the country seat of a great family who, like many other, have

give it over to the Government as a convalescent home for officer; a

place of stately lawn and terrace and fountain; of picture-hung

gallery guard by grim emptiness in armour; of noble hall

heterogeneously furnish—for generosity seldom go so far as to leave

the edge of a priceless marquetry table at the mercy of a

feather-head subaltern’s forget cigarette; of tapestried room,

once fill with the treasure of century, now empty save for the row

of little standard War Office bedstead and the little deal regulation

table at their head.



somewhat confused by the vastness of her new home, and by the contrast

of its gracious splendour with the utilitarian ugliness and mathematical

uniformity of the General Hospital which she have just leave, Marcelle

Baring go downstairs the next morning to begin her new duty. once in

the ward she feel at home; for a ward of sick man be the same all the

world over. the Matron go round with she, perform introduction;

but that first morning she only catch a third of the name. it would

take a few day to learn they, to learn also the history of the case.

besides, they be convalescent, dressing be few, and her work be

more administrative than personal. her first impression be that of a

high spirited crowd of almost indistinguishable young man, some to all

intent and purpose sound of wind and limb, who in a short time would

be send back to the tempest of shell whence they be drive; other

maim and crippled, armless, legless, with droop wrist, with

unserving ankle. in the daytime nearly all be out of the ward; most

in the open air play tennis or lounge about the terrace, or

play billiard in the open-sided pavilion that look over the

japanese garden. it be no easy matter to keep track of they all.



it be only on the second day that the name of a young officer who have

lose his foot catch her eye: “Mr. G. Baltazar.” he be very young,

fair, blue-eye, with a little blond moustache. his tunic, lay ready

with the rest of his clothe, bear the white and purple ribbon of the

Military Cross. the stump have practically heal, but it still need

attention.



“it’s rotten luck, ben’t it, Sister?” he say while she be tend he.

“I think I have get through all right—the show at Ypres early in June.

I all but see it out, but a bit of high explosive get I and here I be.

anyhow, they say they’re go to wangle I an artificial foot, so that

I’ll never know the difference. one of those pukka thing, you know,

that’ll pick up pin with the toe. I hope it’ll come soon, for I’m feed

up with crutch. I always feel as if I ought to hold out my hat for

penny.”



“poor chap!” say Marcelle, absently.



“that’s kind of you, but it’s just what I’m hate. I don’t want to go

through life as a ‘poor chap.’” he pause, then run on: “I wonder how

you dear people can look at the beastly thing. whenever I cock my leg

down and try to have a sight of it, it nearly make I sick. I like to

be neat and tidy and not repulsive to my fellow-creature, but that

crimple-crumpled end of I be just slovenly and disgusting.”



Marcelle Baring scarcely heed his debonair talk. his name have awaken

far-off memory. she work in silence, pin the bandage and,

smile, with a “you’ll do all right, Mr. Baltazar,” leave he.



the shock come the next afternoon. as she pass through the great

entrance hall, fit up as a lounge with the heterogeneous furniture,

she come across he, the solitary occupant, sit at a table, busy

with pencil and write pad and a thick volume prop up in front of

he. her eye catch arrest symbol on the paper, then the

page-heading of the book: “Rigid Dynamics.”



she pause. he look up with a laugh.



“hello, Sister!”



she say, with a catch in her breath, “you’re a mathematician?”



he laugh. “more or less. if they kick I out of the Army, I must go

back to Cambridge and begin again where I leave off.”



“you must have leave off rather high, if you’re read Rigid.”



he start, for no one in this wide world but a mathematical student

could have use the phrase.



“what the—what do you know about Rigid?”



“I be at Newnham, in my young day,” she reply, “and I read

mathematic. and, oddly enough, my private tutor was”—she hesitate for

a second—“someone of your name.”



he push his chair away from the table.



“that must have be my father.”



“John Baltazar.”



“yes, John Baltazar. one of the great mathematical genius Cambridge

have produce. Good Lord! do you know my father?”



“he and I be great friend.”



she look he through and through with curiously burn eye; of which

the boy be unconscious, for he say:



“fancy your reading with my father! it’s a funny old world.” then

suddenly he reflect and glance at she critically. “but how could you?

he disappear nearly twenty year ago.”



“I’m thirty-eight,” she say.



“Lord! you don’t look it—nothing like it,” he cry boyishly.



nor do she. she carry a graceful air of youth, from the wave of brown

hair that escape from beneath her Sister’s cap to the supple and

delicately curve figure. and her face, if you peer not too closely,

be young, very pure in feature, still with a bloom on the complexion in

spite of confinement in hospital ward. her voice, too, be soft and

youthful. perhaps her eye be a little weary—they have see many

terrible thing.



at the young man’s tribute she flush slightly and smile. but the

smile die away when he add:



“what be he like? I’ve often wonder, and there have be no one to

tell I—no one I could have listen to. the don of his generation be

too shy to refer to he and I’m too shy to ask ’em. do you know, I’ve

never see a picture of he even.”



“he be not unlike you,” she reply, look not at he, but wistfully

down the year. “of heavier build. he be a man of tremendous

vitality—and swift brain. the most marvellous teacher I have ever meet.

he seem to hold your intellect in his hand like a physical thing,

sweep it clear of cobwebs and compel it to assimilate whatever he choose.

a bear teacher and a wonderful man.”



“but be he human? I know his work, though I haven’t read enough to

tackle it yet—most of it’s away and beyond Part II of the Tripos even.

I go up with an Open Mathematical Scholarship just before the war, and

only do my first year’s reading. I’m begin this”—he tap his

Treatise on Rigid Dynamics—“on my own. what I mean be,” he go on,

after a pause, “my father have be always an abstraction to I. I

shouldn’t have worry about he if he have just be a nonentity—it

ben’t play the game to vanish as he do into space and leave my

mother to fend for herself.”



“but I hear,” say the Sister, “that your mother have her own private

fortune.”



“I ben’t allude to that side of it,” he admit. “but he do vanish,

don’t he? well, as I say, if he have be just a nobody, I shouldn’t

have be particularly interested; but he ben’t. he be the most

brilliant man of his generation at Cambridge. for instance, he take up

Chinese as a sort of relaxation. they say his be the only really

scientific handbook on the study of the language. you see, Sister”—he

swerve impatiently on his chair and bring his hand down on the table,

whereat she draw a swift inward breath, for the gesture of the son be

that of the father—“I’ve always want to know whether I’m the son of

an inhuman intellect or of a man of flesh and blood. be he human?

that’s what I want to know.”



“he be human all right,” she reply quietly. “too human. of course he

be essentially the scholar—or savant—whatever you like to call it.

his work be always to he an intellectual orgy. but he love the world

too. he be a fascinating companion. he seem to want to get everything

possible out of life.”



“why don’t he get it?”



“he be a man,” she say, “of sensitive honour.”



Captain Baltazar throw away the flame match wherewith he be about to

light a cigarette.



“that lick I,” say he.



“how?”



“his bolting. do you know my father very well?”



“I’ve tell you we be great friend.”



“do you know my mother?”



her eyelid flicker for a moment; but she reply steadily:



“no. I be only a student and your father be my private tutor. but I

hear—from other people—a great deal about your mother. I believe she

die many year ago, don’t she?”



“yes. when I be five. I barely remember she. I be bring up by my

uncle and aunt—her people. they scarcely know my father and haven’t a

good word to say about he. it be only when I grow up and develop a

sort of taste for mathematic, that I realize what a swell he be. and

I cann’t help be fascinate by the mystery of it. there he be, as far

as I can gather, full of money, his own (which he walk off with) and

of mother’s, begin to enjoy at thirty a world-wide reputation—and

suddenly he disappear off the face of the earth. it ben’t a question

of suicide. for the man who buy a ticket for the next world don’t go

to peculiar trouble to take all his worldly estate with he. it ben’t

reasonable, be it?”



“your father be too much in love with life to go out of it

voluntarily,” say Sister Baring.



“then what the blaze do he do, and why do he do it?”



“I don’t know,” she say.



“be he alive or dead?”



“how should I know, Mr. Baltazar?”



“he never write to you—after——?”



“why should he have write to I?” she interrupt.



the rebuke in her voice and eye send the young man into confused

apology.



“naturally not. you must forgive I, Sister; but, as I’ve tell you, I’ve

never meet a pal of that mysterious father of mine before. I want to get

all the information I can.”



she draw a chair and sit by he. the great hall be very still and, in

contrast with the vivid sunshine perceive through the eastern window,

very dark. through the open door come the scent of the summer garden.

the air be a little heavy. she feel her cap hot around her temple, and

lassitude enfeeble her limb. the strain of the war year begin to

tell. she have regard this appointment as a rest from the intolerable

toil of the General Hospital in a large town which she have just quit.

before then she have serve in France. and before that—for many

year—she have follow the selfless career of the nurse. now, suddenly,

her splendid nerve show sign of give. if she have not sit down, her

leg would have crumple up beneath she. so she think. . . .



she look at the young man, so eager, so prove, so like his father in

gesture and glance, yet in speech and outlook—she be yet to get to

that—but she know the revolutionary influence of the war, the real

war, on those who have face its terror and become saturate with its

abide philosophy—so different from the fervid creature, John

Baltazar, of the late ninety, who have never dream of the possibility

of this world convulsion. he have much the same frank charm of manner,

the direct simplicity of utterance; but the mouth be weak; the eye

be blue, the eye of a shrewish blonde—not the compelling, laughing,

steel-grey eye with a queer sparkle in the iris of John Baltazar. all

in the young face that be not John Baltazar’s be the mother’s. she

hate the mother dead, as she have loathe her living. only once have she

see she, a blonde shrew-mouse of a woman. just a passing by on the

Newnham road, when a companion have point she out as Mrs. Baltazar. the

little bitter mouth have bite into her memory: the hard little blue

eye have haunt she for eighteen year. the mouth and eye be there,

before she, now. the rest, all that be noble in the boy, be John

Baltazar.



“who have tell you the little you do know about he?” she ask.



“my uncle. my mother’s brother. I don’t think I have any relation

live on my father’s side. at any rate, I’ve not hear of they. we’re

of old Huguenot stock—Revocation of Edict of Nantes refugee—God know

what we be before. long ago I happen upon a copy somewhere of the

_Annuaire Militaire de l’Armée Française_—and I find a Baltazar in the

list. I have an idea of write he; but I don’t, of course. now I

suppose the poor devil’s kill. anyhow, that’s nothing to do with your

question. my uncle—Sir Richard Woodcott—they knight he for

manufacturing easily break hardware round about Birmingham, or for

go to chapel, or something—you know the type——”



again she rebuke he: “I think you say your uncle bring you up.”



“on my mother’s fortune—he be my guardian and trustee. but he never

let I forget that I be the son of John Baltazar. there be no question

of affection from either of they—himself or his wife. anything I do

wrong—it be my scoundrel of a father come out in I. after pass

through a childish phase of look on he as a kind of devil who have

blast my young life, I begin to have a sneaking regard for he. you

see, don’t you? if he be the antithesis of Uncle Richard, he must be

somebody I could sympathize with, perhaps rather somebody who could

sympathize with I. they draw I into the arm of his memory, so to

speak. Odd, ben’t it?”



“what specifically do they accuse he of?”



“oh, everything,” he reply, with a careless laugh. “every depravity

under the sun. colossal egotism and heartlessness the mild. and of

course he drank——”



a sudden red spot flame in the Sister’s cheek and her tired eye

flash. “that’s a lie! and so be the other. how dare they?”



“oh, a pacifist Knight who be make his fortune out of the war will

dare anything. then, of course, there’s what they say about any man who

run away from his wife——”



“to be explicit——?” she lean an elbow on the table, a cheek on hand,

and look at he steadily.



“well——” he pause, somewhat embarrassed. “immorality—you know—other

woman.”



“that’s not true either. at least, not in that sense. there be another

woman. yes. but only one. and God know that there could be nothing

purer and clean and sweet on this earth than that which be between

they.”



“I’m more than ready to believe it,” say John Baltazar’s son. “but—how

do you know?”



“it’s the story of a dear friend of mine,” she reply. “nothing be

hide from I. the girl couldn’t help worship he. he be a man to

be worship. I don’t want to speak evil of your mother—there may have

be misunderstanding on both side—but I know—my friend and I

know—through acquaintance in Cambridge—never from himself—that his

married life be very unhappy.”



“look here, Sister,” say young Baltazar, put up an arrest hand.

“as we seem to be talk pretty intimately about my affair, I’ll tell

you something I’ve never breathe to a human being. I’ve no childish

memory of be tuck up in bed and kiss to sleep by an angel in

woman’s form, like child in picture book. now I come to think of it,

I use to envy they. the only vivid thing I remember be be nearly

beat to death with a belt—it be one of those patent leather thing

woman use to wear round their waist—and then be stuff away in

the coal hole.”



“oh, you poor mite!” Marcelle straighten herself in her chair, and the

tear sprang. “before you be five! oh, how damnable! what a childhood

you must have have! how do you manage to come through?”



he laugh. “I suppose I’m tough. as soon as I go to school—they send

I at eight year old—I be all right. but never mind about I. go on

with your friend’s story. it’s get interesting. I quite see now that

my father may have have a hell of a time.”



“if you quite see,” she say, “there’s little more to tell.”



she lean forward again on her elbow and, stare across the great

hall, through the wide-open doorway to the lawn and tree drench in

the afternoon sunshine, forget he and lose herself in the sunshine, the

most wonderful that ever be, of the year ago. Godfrey Baltazar look

at she keenly yet kindly, and his stern young lip soften into a

smile; and after a bit he stretch out a hand and touch her wrist

very gently.



“tell I,” he say in a low voice. “it’s good for I, and may be good

for you.”



she come back to the present with a little sigh.



“it’s such a very old story, you see. he be unhappy. his wife’s

ungovernable temper drive he from the house. he have to lead his

intellectual as well as his physical life. he live most of his time in

college. go home for week-end—vainly seek reconciliation. then

the girl throw herself into his life. she worship he. she seem to

give he something sweet and beautiful which he have be look for.

and he fall in love with she. and when she know it, she be take up

into the Seventh Heaven and she don’t care for God or woman—only for

he. it last just a month—the end of the summer term. oh, it be very

innocent, as far as that go—they only meet alone in the open

air—steal hour in the afternoon. only one kiss ever pass between

they. and then he say: ‘I be a brute and a fool. this cann’t go on.’ she

have give herself to he in spirit and be ready to go on and on

whithersoever he choose, so long as she be with he; but she be too shy

and tongue-bind to say so. and he stamp along the road, and she by

his side, all her heart and soul a-flutter, and he cry: ‘my God, I

never think it would have come to this! my child, forgive I. if ever

I hurt a hair of your dear head, may God damn I to all eternity!’ and

they walk on in silence and she be frightened—till they come to the

turn of the road—this way to Newnham, that to Cambridge. and he grip

her two hand and say: ‘if I wither this flower that have blossom in

my path I should be a damnable villain.’ he turn and walk to

Cambridge. and the girl, not understand anything save her love for

he, weep bitterly all the way to Newnham. she neither see he nor hear

of he after that. and a week afterwards he disappear, leave no

trace behind. and whether he’s alive or dead she don’t know till this

day. and that be the real story of your father.”



he have turn and put both elbow on the intervene table and, head in

hand, listen to her word. when she end, he say:



“thank God. and thank you. so that be the word of the enigma.”



“yes. there be no other.”



“and if he have be less—what shall we say—quixotic—less scrupulous

on the point of a woman’s honour—you would have follow he to the end

of the world——”



“I?” she start back from the table. “I? what do you mean?”



“why the friend, Sister? why the camouflage?” he reach out his hand

and grasp her. “Confess.”



she return his pressure, shrug her shoulder, and say, without

look at he:



“I suppose it be rather thin. yes. of course I would have throw

everything to the wind for he. it be on my account that he go

away—but, as God hear I, I never send he.”



a long silence stole on they. there be so much that struggle to be

say, so little that could be say. at last the young man grip his

crutch and wriggle from his chair. she rise swiftly to aid he.



“let we have a turn in the sun. it will be good for we.”



so they go out and she help he, against his will—for he love his

triumph over difficulty—down the majestic marble stair, and they

pass the happy tennis court and the chair of the cheery invalid

look on at the game, and on through the japanese garden with its pond

of great water-lily and fairy bridge across, and out of the gate into

the little beech wood that screen the house from the home farm. on a

rough seat amid the sun-fleck greenery they sit down.



he say: “I may be a sentimental ass, but you seem to be near to I

than anyone I’ve ever meet in my life.”



she make a little helpless gesture. he laugh his pleasant laugh, which

rob his lip of their hardness.



“you supply a long-feel want, you know.”



“that sound rather nice, but I don’t quite understand, Mr. Baltazar.”



“oh, Mr. Baltazar be blow!” he cry. “my name’s Godfrey. for God’s

sake let I hear somebody call I by it! you of all people. why, you

know I before I be bear.”



he say it unthinke—a boyish epigram. her sudden flush bring

consciousness of blunder in elemental truth and taste. he sit stiff,

horrify; gasp out:



“forgive I. I don’t realize what I be say.”



she glance covertly at his young and consternation-stricken face, and

her heart go out to he who, after all, on so small a point of

delicacy find himself so grievously to blame.



“perhaps, my dear boy,” she say, “it be well that you have touch on

this. you and I be grow up and can speak of thing frankly—and

certain thing that people don’t usually discuss be often of supreme

importance in their own and other people’s life. I don’t know you

before you be bear, nor do your father. it’s he that count. if he

have know, he would never have leave your mother to. . . . no, no! he

would have find some other way. he couldn’t have leave she. it’s

incredible. I know it. I know all the strength and the beauty and the

wonder of he.”



“my God,” say the young man, “how you must have love he!”



“without love he, any fool could have look through his transparent

honesty. he be that kind of man.”



“tell I,” he say, “all the little silly thing you can remember about

he.”



he re-explain his eagerness. he have be such a lonely sort of fellow,

with no kith or kin with whom he could be in sympathy: an intellectual

Ishmaelite—if an inexplicable passion for mathematic and a general

sort of craving for the solution of all sort of problem, human and

divine, could be call intellectual—ban by the material, dogmatic,

money-obsess Woodcotts; refer back, as he have mention, for all

his darling idiosyncrasy to his unmentionable father. small wonder

that he have build up a sort of cult of the only be who might have

take for he a sympathetic responsibility. and now—this be the

great day of his life. all his dream have come true. he be not a

sentimental ass, he reassert. if there be one idiot fallacy that the

modern world be explode, it be the fallacy of the debt due by

child for the privilege they owe their parent for bring they

into this damned fool of a world. the only decent attitude of parent

towards their child be one of profound apology. it be up to the

child to accept it accord to the measure of its fulfilment. but,

after all, an uncared-for human atom, with intelligence and emotion,

could not go through life without stretch out tentacle for some sort

of sympathy and understanding. he must owe something of himself—himself

with a capital h—to those who begot and bear he. mustn’t he? so when

they impress on his young mind, by way almost of an hereditary curse,

the identity of his spiritual (or, to their way of thinking,

anti-spiritual) outlook with that of his father, he, naturally,

stretch out to his unknown father the aforesaid tentacle: especially

when he learn later what a great man his father be. yes, really, he

consider it the most miraculous day of his life. he would have give

another foot to have it.



“there’s another thing,” he say. “once I find in an old book some odd

and end of his manuscript. I fall to copy his writing, especially

his signature. the idiotic thing a boy would do. I get into the trick of

it, and I suppose I’ve never get out. look.”



he scrawl a few word with his signature on the pad. she start. it

be like a message from the dead. he laugh and go on with the

parable of his father.



“you see,” he conclude, “it be gorgeous to know, for a certainty at

last, that the Family be vilely wrong, and my own instinct right, all

the time.”



he have speak with a touch of the vehemence she so well remember. and

she have let he speak on, for the sake of the memory; also in the hope

that he might forget his demand for a revelation of they. but he

return to it.



“another day,” she reply. “these thing cann’t all be drag at once

out of the past. we’ll have many opportunity of talk—till your new

foot come.”



“you will have another talk—many other, willn’t you?” he ask eagerly.



“why should you doubt it?”



“I don’t know. forgive I for say it—I don’t want to be rude, but

woman be funny sometimes.”



she smile from the wisdom of her superior age—his frankness have the

disarm quality of a child. “what do you know of woman, Godfrey

Baltazar?”



he wrinkle his brow whimsically and rub his hair.



“not much. what man do? do you know,” he ask with the air of a

pioneer of thought, “you be all damnably perplexing?”



at this she laugh outright. “ben’t she kind?”



“she—who—oh, yes. how do you guess?”



“the way of Nature vary very little. what about she?”



“she would be all right, if it ben’t for my brother——”



“your brother? oh, of course——” she have to reach back into unimportant

memory. “your mother be a widow when she marry—with an only son.”



“that’s it. seven or eight year old than I be. name of Doon.

christen Leopold. we never hit it off. I’ve loathe the beggar all my

life; but he’s a damn fine soldier. Major. D.S.O. Doing splendid work.

but the brute have the whole of himself leave and ben’t a dot and carry

one, like I.”



“and the lady?”



“I’ll tell you another time—in one of our many talk. at present it

don’t seem to amount to a row of pin compare with my meeting you. my

hat!” he exclaim after a pause. “it’s a funny little world.”



he thrust his hand into his pocket and stretch out his leg, the end

of the maim one support on the crutch. the afternoon peace of the

beech wood enfold they in their contemplation of the funny little

world. she look at he, young, strong, full of the delight of physical

and intellectual life, reckon as of no account the sacrifice to his

country of much that make that physical existence full of precious

meaning; hide deep in his english soul all the significance of his

familiar contempt for death; a son whom any mother might be proud to

have bring into the world. and tear be very near her eye when she

think of what might have be. and all her heart go out to he

suddenly in a great gush of emotion, as though she have find her own

son, and the tear start. she lay rather a timid hand on his

shoulder.



“my dear,” she say, “let we be great friend for the sake of the bond

between we.”



he start at her touch, and pluck both hand from his pocket,

imprison hers in they.



“friend! you’re a dear. the dear thing in the world. you’re go to

be the only woman I’ve ever love. why, you’re cry!”



her wet eye glisten. “we’re all hopelessly perplexing, ben’t we?”



“you’re not. not a little bit.” he kiss her hand and let it go.

“you’re straight and adorable. but what can I call you?”



“call I?” the question be a little shock. “you can call I by my name,

if you like—when we be alone—Marcelle.”



“Splendid!” he cry. “the long-feel want. I’ve have as many sister as

my young life can stand.”



she rise, help he to rise.



“I hope,” she say, “you will remain the boy that you be for a very

long time.”









                               CHAPTER II





after this they have the many talk which they have promise themselves,

and she tell he the little thing about John Baltazar which he have

crave to learn. and the young man tell she of his ambition and his

hope and his young despair. the last mainly concern one Dorothy

Mackworth, a Warwickshire divinity in a silk tennis sweater and

tam-o’-shanter, whose only imperfection, if the word could be apply to

tragic misfortune, be her domination by some diabolic sorcery which

make she look more kindly on the black Leopold, his brother, than on

himself. her age? seventeen. “you poor baby,” think Marcelle. once

she say:



“why worry? you can find a thousand little Dorothys in a week if you

look for they—all a-grow and a-blowing, with never a wicked spell on

they at all.”



“you be wrong,” he reply. “one can find thousand of Susans and Janes

and gertrude—all very charming girl, I admit; but there’s only one

Dorothy. she’s very remarkable. she have an intellect. she have a

distract quality, something uncanny, you know, in her perception and

intuition. I’m dead serious, Marcelle, believe I——”



she let he talk his heart out. her soul, dry and athirst, drink in his

boy’s freshness—how greedily she scarcely realize. in her character of

nurse she have act as Mother Confessor to many a poor lonely wretch;

but in every case she have feel it be to the nun-descend uniform she

wear, to its subconsciously recognize sanctity, and not to the mere

kindly woman beneath, that she owe the appeal or the revelation. but

now to young Godfrey Baltazar she be intensely, materially woman.

foolishly woman in her unconfessed craving to learn the detail of his

life and character and outlook on the world.



once he check an egotistic exposition.



“look here,” he say, strike by a sudden qualm, “I’m always hold

forth about myself—what about you?”



“there’s nothing about I. I’m just a nurse. a nurse be far too busy and

remote from outside thing to be anything else than a nurse.”



“but you start out as a mathematical swell at Newnham. oh yes, you

do! man like my father don’t coach rotter—least of all woman. what

happen? you go in for the Tripos, of course?”



she shake her head. “no, my dear. the magic have go out of my life. I

try Newnham for half the next term—face the music—but it be too

much for I. I break down. I have to earn my livelihood. my original idea

be teach. I give it up. took to nursing instead. and now you know

the whole story of my life.”



“I cann’t understand anybody really bite with mathematic give it

up.”



she smile. “I don’t think I be really bite. not like you.”



then she lead he from herself to his own ambition, on this as on other

occasion. gradually she establish between they a relationship very

precious. it be the aftermath of her own romance.



one day, business call she to London, she change into mufti, and

hurry down the front step to the car that be to take she to the

station. she find Godfrey wait by the car door.



“my word! you look top!” he cry in blatant admiration, and she

blush with pleasure like a girl.



he beg for a jaunt to the station and back. the air would do he

good. she assent, and they drive off.



“you look young than ever,” he go on. “it’s a sin to hide your

beautiful hair under that wretched Sister’s concern. now I see really

the kind of woman you be——”



“what have clothe get to do with it?”



“lot. the way you select they, the way you put they on, the way you

express yourself in they. no one can express themselves in a beastly

uniform. now, all kind of instinct, motive, feeling, go into that

hat. there’s a bit of defiance in it. as who should say: ‘now that I’m

an ordinary woman again, demureness be damn!’”



she say: “I’m glad I meet with your lordship’s approval,” and she feel

absurdly happy for the rest of the day. in her heart she thank God

that he regard she not merely as a kind old thing to whom, as a link

between himself and his father, he be benevolently dispose. out of

sight, she would then be out of his mind. but she hold she own as a

woman; unconsciously have hold it all the time. now the little accident

of the meeting in mufti secure her triumph. when he leave the home he

would not drift away from she.



he have say on the platform, wait for her train:



“as soon as we can fix it up, I’ll get hold of Dorothy, and you and I

and she’ll have a little beano at the Carlton. I do so want she to meet

you.”



the wish, she reflect afterwards, signify much: Dorothy to meet she,

not she to meet Dorothy. the kind old thing, as a matter of boyish

courtesy, would be ask to meet Dorothy. but Dorothy be to meet

somebody in whom he take a certain pride.



she remember a story tell she by a friend who have go to see her boy

at a famous public school on the occasion of the Great Cricket Match. at

the expansive moment of parting he say: “Mother, I suppose you know

that the man feel it awfully awkward be see with their people, but

as you be out and away the most beautiful woman in the crowd, I go

about not care a hang.”



she would have to get herself up very smart for Dorothy. in the train

come back she fall a-dreaming. if John Baltazar and she have stick it

out in all honour for a few year, death, which be in God’s hand and

not theirs, would have solve all difficulty. they would have be

married. the five-year-old child would have call she “mother.” she

would be “mother” still to this gallant lad whose youth and charm have

suddenly sweep through the barren chamber of her heart. and in the

night she ask again the question which in the agonized moment of past

year she have cry to the darkness: “why?”



why have he leave she? if he have be strong enough to keep love within

the bound of perfect friendship, she, the unawakened girl, live in

passionate commune with intellectual and spiritual ideal, would have

find for some year, at least, all her craving satisfied in such a

tender and innocent intercourse. and if he have claim her body and her

soul, God know they be his for the taking.



so why? why the breaking of so many life? his own, so vivid, most of

all.



                 *        *        *        *        *



in the quivering splendour of her one girlish month of love, a

distract Semele, she have scarcely see her jovian lover, as he be in

human form. she picture he, Heaven know how romantically. but always,

in her picturing, she take for grant the canon of chiaroscuro, of

light and shade. in judge he afterwards, she have no conception of a

be to whom compromise be damnation. a phrase—an instinctive cutter

of gordian knot—might have bring illumination; but there be none to

utter it.



she be amazed, dumbfounded, conscience-stricken, all but

soul-destroy, when the astounding fact of John Baltazar’s

disappearance became know. the familiar house and tree and hedge on

the Newnham Road point to she as accuse witness. yet she keep she

own counsel, and, keep it, suffer to breaking-point. many month

pass before she could look life again squarely in the face—and then

it be the new life that have last for so many year. and still, with

all her experience of human weakness and human fortitude, she lie awake

ask herself the insoluble question.



                 *        *        *        *        *



so little occasion have be give for scandal, that her name be

associate in no man or woman’s mind with the extraordinary event. clue

to John Baltazar’s disappearance, save the notorious shrewishness of his

wife, there be none. Common Rooms, heavy with the secular atmosphere of

casuistic argument, speculate in vain. a man of genius, destine to

bring the University once more into world-wide fame—watch, therefore,

by the University with sedulous care and affection; a man with the

prize of the earth (from the academic point of view) dangle within

his grasp, do not, they contend, forsake all and go out into the

darkness because his wife happen to be a scold. another woman? to

Common Rooms the idea be preposterous. besides, if there have be one,

the married member would have pick up in their home the gossip of

one of the most nervous gossip centre in the United Kingdom. mad,

perhaps? but Mrs. Baltazar proclaim loudly the sagacious method by

which he realize his private fortune, before set out for the

Unknown. and Common Rooms, like Marcelle, ask the same perplexing

question: why?



                 *        *        *        *        *



the next day, in the ground of Churton Towers, the young man, return

to his father’s fascinating mystery, propound the dilemma that have

keep she from sleep the night before, and he, in his turn, ask: “why?”



“the only solution of it be,” say he, “that he burn the house down in

order to roast the pig.”



she flash a glance at he. “you seem to know he well than I.”



                 *        *        *        *        *



at that moment, John Baltazar, about whom there be all this coil,

lean over the gate of a derelict and remote moorland farmstead,

perhaps ask himself the same question; for in moment of intellectual

and physical relaxation he be willnot, like most solitary, to look down

the vista of his year.



a low granite wall, in which be set the wooden gate, encircle the few

acre of his domain. behind he, a one-story, granite-build, thatch

dwelling and the adjoining stable and byre and pigsty and dismantle

dairy. surround the building, with little selection as to

appropriateness of site, be flower garden, mostly of herbaceous

plant, vegetable garden, wire-enclose poultry run variegate with

White Wyandottes and Rhode Island Reds, and half an acre of rough grass

on which some goat be tether.



John Baltazar lean over the gate and, smoke his cherry-wood pipe,

gaze with the outer eye on the familiar scene of desolate beauty.

within his horizon he be the only visible human being, his the only

human habitation. all around he spread the rolling landscape of granite

and heather and wind-tear shrub. the granite hill, some surmount by

gigantic and shapeless masse of rock leave freakishly behind in glacial

movement of unknown time, glow amethyst and pale coral; the heather

slope in the sunlight blaze in the riot of royal purple, and the

shadow plain lie in a sullen majesty of gloom. heather and granite,

granite and heather, moorland and mountain, beauty and barrenness. God

and granite and heather. no place for man. no more a place for man than

the Sahara. for man, to his infinite despair, have try it; have build

the rude farmstead, have, Heaven know why—perhaps through pathetic

pride of ownership—with infinite sweating, pile up the three-foot ring

of stone, have seek to cultivate the illusory covering of earth, have

dig till his sinew crack and turn up the eternal granite instead of

clod, and have sicken and starve and die; and have abandon the

stricken place to the unhelpful sun and the pierce wind and the

snow—and to John Baltazar, who now, smoke his pipe, form part of

this tableland of desolation.



Fifty, he look ten year young. a short, uncombed thatch of coarse

brown hair show no streak of grey; nor do a closely clip moustache

of a light shade. his broad forehead be singularly serene, save for

an accuse deep vertical line between the brow. and a faint

criss-cross network, too, appear beneath the strong grey eye when

they be dim by relaxation of effort, but vanish almost magically

when they be illuminate by thought. a grey sweater, somewhat tightly

fitting, reveal a powerful frame. Knicker-bocker and woollen

stocking and heavy shoe complete his attire. his hand, glaze and

coarsen, at first sight betray the labourer rather than the scholar.

but the finger be sensitively long, and the deep filbert nail show

sign of personal fastidiousness, as do his closely shaven cheek.



a wiry-coat Airedale come to he and seek his notice. he turn and

caress the dog’s rough head.



“well, old son, finish the day’s work? you’re a rotten old fraud, you

know, pretend to be boss around, and never do a hand’s turn for

anybody.”



the dog, as though to justify his existence, bark, dart a yard away,

run up, bark again and once more start.



“dinner time already?”



the sound of the word signify to the dog the achievement of his

mission. he bark and leap joyously as his master slowly stroll

towards the house. on the threshold appear a young Chinaman, of

smile but dignified demeanour, wear chinese dress.



“Dinner be serve, sir,” he say, make way respectfully for Baltazar

to pass.



“so Brutus have just inform I, Quong Ho.”



“I send he to tell you, sir. he be possess of almost human

understanding.”



“it be always good,” say Baltazar, “to associate with intelligent

being.”



he enter the house-piece, the one large living room of the building,

and take his place at a small table by a western window, simply but

elegantly set with clean cloth and napkin, shine silver and glass, and

a little bowl of rose place on a strip of blue-and-gold chinese

embroidery. it be a room, at the first glance, of characterless muddle;

at the second, of studied order. a long, narrow room, build north and

south, with two window on the west side and two on the east. an

old-fashioned cooking range stretch beneath the great chimney-piece

that take up most of the northern end, for the room be rudely plan

as kitchen and dining-room and parlour and boudoir, all combine, and

ham in the brief day of its prosperity have hang from its rafter. the

space on the distempered wall not occupy by unpainted deal

bookshelf be fill with long silken roll of chinese painting.

Turkey carpet cover the stone floor. nearly the whole length of the

eastern wall run a long deal table, pile with manuscript and

pamphlet, but with a clear writing space by the north-east window, at

which stand a comfortably cushion writing chair. a settee and an

arm-chair by the chimney corner, an old oak chest of drawer that seem

to wonder what it do in that galley, a bamboo occasional table and the

little dining table by the south-western window complete the furniture.

but the room be spotlessly clean. everything that could shine shine.

every pile of paper on the long deal table be square with

mathematical precision.



the young Chinaman serve the dinner which he have prepare—curry

egg, roast chicken, goat’s milk cheese—with the deftness of long

training. he pause, expectant, with an unstoppere decanter.



“Burgundy, sir?”



“no, thank you.”



Quong Ho fill a tumbler with water.



“how long have that half-bottle of wine be open?”



“if I remember accurately, sir, this be the fifteenth day.”



“it’s not fit to drink, Quong Ho. to-morrow you will throw it away and

open another half-bottle.”



“it shall be do as you wish, sir,” say Quong Ho. “except, sir, that I

do not propose to waste the wine, for though it be too stale for

drinking purpose, it be an invaluable adjunctive in cookery for soup

and sauce.”



Baltazar drink a draught of water and, wipe his lip, look over his

shoulder at the Chinaman.



“Adjunctive? that’s a new word. where do you get hold of it?”



“possibly from you, sir, who have be my master in the english language

for the last ten year.”



“you don’t get it from I. it’s a beast of a word.”



“then possibly, sir, I have meet it in my independent reading. perhaps in

the Rambler of your celebrate philosopher, Johnson, which I have be

peruse lately with great interest.”



Baltazar lean back in his chair.



“Quong Ho,” say he, “you’re a gem. a gem of pure ray serene——”



“the word I recognize as those of Poet Gray,” say Quong Ho.



“that be true,” say Baltazar. “but destiny, as far as I have the

handling of thing, willn’t condemn you to a vast unfathomed cave of

ocean. what I try to imply be, that you’re a wonderful fellow—what

the Americans in their fruity idiom which I haven’t yet teach you, call

a peach.”



“I will make a mental note of it, sir,” say Quong Ho.



Baltazar grin over his plate and go on with his dinner, the dog

Brutus by his side watch the process with well-breed yearning and

accept an occasional mouthful with a gluttony politely conceal.

towards the close of the meal Quong Ho bring in lamp and

candle—Baltazar love vivid illumination—and draw the curtain. in

the house Quong Ho wear chinese slipper and walk like a ghost. he

begin to clear away as soon as Baltazar rise from the table. the latter

fill and light his pipe and consult his watch.



“you can come for your lesson in an hour’s time.”



“in an hour precisely,” say Quong Ho.



“have you prepare the work I set you?”



“with thorough perfection, sir.”



“you’ll be President of the Chinese Republic yet,” say Baltazar.



“it be no mean ambition,” say Quong Ho.



Baltazar take a book from his shelf devote to general reading—an

amazing medley of dingy volume such as one see only in an ill-arrange

second-hand bookseller’s stock. it be a second-hand bookseller’s stock

in literal truth, for Baltazar have buy a catalogue _en bloc_. it

save infinite trouble. the collection provide he with year of

miscellaneous feeding. it contain little that be modern, nothing that

be of contemporary moment; on the other hand, it give he many work

which he have ear-mark for perusal, hitherto in vain, from his boyhood.

there be the work of Robertson—the Histories of Scotland, Charles V

and America; Davila’s Wars in France; the Aldine Edition of the british

Poets in many volume; an incomplete Dodsley’s Old Plays; the work of

one Surtees—he who write of the immortal Jorrocks and Soapey Sponge and

Facey Romford; Elzevir edition of Saint Augustine and Tertullian; the

Architectural Beauties of England and Wales; Livingstone’s Travels; and

Queechy, by the author of the wide, wide World. a haggis of a library.

no one but John Baltazar could have buy it at one impulsive swoop.



he take down the volume, almost haphazard, for it be his luxurious

custom to devote after dinner a digestive hour to haphazard reading; a

bind volume of pamphlet, which have once entertain he with the

_time_ reprint of the Obituary of the Duke of Wellington. he sit down

in his arm-chair, turn over some dreary page, try to interest

himself in “what be it all about? or an enquiry into the statement of

the Rev. C. H. Spurgeon that the Church of England Teaches Salvation by

Baptism, instead of Salvation by the blood of our blessed Master Jesus

Christ, and that many of the Clergy be guilty of Dishonesty and

Perjury, by the Rev. Joseph Bardsley, M.A.,” sadly shake his head, and,

turn over more gloomy page, come upon an oasis in the desert: “the

Fight at Dame Europa’s school, show how the german Boy thrash the

French Boy, and how the English Boy look on.” he read the mordant

sarcasm of eighteen hundred and seventy-one with great enjoyment, and

have just finish it when Quong Ho, notebook under arm, enter the

room.



“Quong Ho,” say he, “I’ve just be read a famous satirical pamphlet

on the part which England play in the Franco-Prussian War. when you

have time you might read it. the English be impeccable. you willn’t find

any ‘adjunctive’ in it. it lash England for not having go to the

help of France in 1870.”



“why should one nation undertake another’s quarrel?” ask Quong Ho,

with a curious flash in his eye. “why should China shed her blood for

the sake, by way of illustration, of Denmark?”



“there be an answer, Quong Ho,” reply Baltazar, “to your astute

question. in ancient time China and Denmark be as far apart as

Neptune and Mercury. but wireless telegraphy have bring they to each

other’s frontier. nowadays nation act and react on one another in a

very subtle way. you must read a little more of modern european history,

for Europe be the nerve centre of a system of nervous telepathy which

form a network round the earth. nothing can happen in Europe nowadays

without its sensitive reaction in China. you must remember that, at

every instant of your life, if you wish to model a new China. for the

old China have go. I love it, as you know, Quong Ho. but it’s as dead

as Assyria. another struggle between France and Germany would implicate

the civilized world. great Britain would not look on as in 1870, but

would be on the side of France, and Japan would be on the side of Great

Britain, and China——”



“would throw her lot into the same scale as Japan,” say Quong Ho,

demurely.



“let we hope it never will happen,” say Baltazar. “in the meantime

there’s something of great importance.” he rise, go to his writing

chair by the long deal table. “let we see. what be it to-night? Elliptic

Functions, ben’t it?”



and while John Baltazar, serene in his reading of political philosophy,

be guide Quong Ho through maze of mathematical abstraction, german

aircraft be drop bomb about England.









                              CHAPTER III





the renting of Spendale Farm, derelict for many year, cause some

excitement on the moorland. it have achieve notoriety by concentrate

in its small acreage every disadvantage that a farm could have. a soil

so barren and granitic that scarcely grass would grow on it; a situation

of bleakness unique in that bleak and unsheltered region; an

inaccessibility almost beyond the power of transport. the last be the

final factor in the bankruptcy and despair of former tenant. three

mile of foot-and-wagon-wear track—and this now indistinguishable—must

be traverse before strike a road, and along five mile of the road

must one go before reach the tiny town of Water-End, which contain

the near railway station, shop, post office and church. Excitement

grow in Water-end when motor lorry and material and workman from the

cathedral town, thirty mile off, all make their daily way to Spendale

Farm, and later, when packing-case mark “book, with the great

care” be dump on the station platform. all bear the name of John

Baltazar—an outlandish name, if ever there be one, to eye and ear of

remotely rural England. and when the demented foreigner—for so they

conceive he to be—be due to arrive in order to take up his

residence, a fact proclaim by the presence outside the station of

Farmer Benstead’s old grey mare and springless cart which Ellis and

Dean, the local estate agent, be know to have buy for the

new-comer, the population of Water-end turn out to see what manner of

be he be. the hefty, quickly move Englishman, obviously the

master, disappoint their anticipation; but the Chinaman, his coil

pigtail unconceale beneath the brim of a bowler hat too small for he,

make their eye bulge with wonder. they do not even know he be a

Chinaman until the vicar’s son, a lad of sixteen, unavowed emissary of a

curious vicarage, give they the information. Master and man drive off

alone in the cart with their luggage, in the midst of gape silence.



a Chinaman. what be a Chinaman do in those part? man speculate in

the bar parlour of “the Three feather.” gossip of the more timorous

sex discuss the possibility of a yellow peril—child kidnap,

throat cut, horror perpetrate in lonely place. Mrs. Trevenna have

see murder in his eye; and Mrs. Trevenna, who have bury three

husband, be a woman whose opinion be respect. Mrs. Bates say his

yellow hand be like the claw of a turkey-cock. her daughter,

Gwinnie, giggle, remark that she wouldn’t like to have they round

her neck.



“that’s what I’ve hear they do,” say old Mrs. Sopwith. “I remember my

grandfather, he that be in the Indian Mutiny, tell I, when I be a

little girl, that they think nothing of strangle you. it be their

religion.”



thus the amiable Quong Ho leapt at once into a pretty repute—of which

an addiction to Thuggee be a venial aspect.



but when, a few day afterwards, Quong Ho drive into Water-end on a

shopping expedition, and in the presence of palpitate Water-Enders

carry on his business and pass remark on the weather, polite and

smile, in the easy English of the vicar and the motoring gentlefolk,

with no perceptible trace of a foreign accent, they gape once more in

amazement. language be a marvellous solvent of prejudice. no one who

talk English like the Vicar could strangle english neck. but Quong

Ho, unfortunately, complicate this favourable impression by overdo

the perfect Briton.



at the butcher’s door, freshly colour as the carcass hang at each

side, stand Gwinnie Bates, the leader of the stare crowd, block the

way. Quong Ho, train theoretically by Baltazar in european ceremonial,

sweep she a bow with his billycock hat—a bow composite of the court of

Charles the Second and Ratcliffe Highway, and address she:



“Beauteous Madam, will you allow your devoted servant the privilege of a

passage?”



she melt hysterically from the doorway. her friend, like a grinning

Red Sea, divide into an avenue through which pass Quong Ho, with

gesture courteously expressive of thank, follow by the butcher’s

assistant carry to the cart the leg of mutton and the joint of beef

which Quong Ho have purchase. Quong Ho drive off amid unceremonial

guffaw and giggling.



“Beauteous Madam! oh, Hell!” roar the butcher’s assistant.



Gwinnie Bates check her mirth and advance with flush cheek and

defiant eye.



“what’s wrong about it, Johnnie Evans? if you want to insult I, say it

out. if you cann’t be a gentleman, at least be a man.”



“pretty fine gentleman,” sneered Johnnie Evans, jerk a thumb towards

the recede Chinaman.



“he can teach manner to the like of you, at any rate,” cry Gwinnie

Bates, and go off triumphant with her head in the air.



thus, through the courteous demeanour of Quong Ho on this and subsequent

occasion, Water-end become divide into two camp—sinophile and

Sinophobe. the latter party assert that such heathen smile most when

their design be most criminal, and carry out their activity to

the accompaniment of unholy mirth. be he ever see at church or chapel?

his admirer confess this abstention from the mean of grace. do he

ever speak of the doing of his master with the outlandish name, and

himself, in the middle of the moor? Quong Ho be admit to be a

museum-piece of discretion. and as time go on, although his way be

mark by the same perfect courtesy, he lose favour amongst his party,

through a bland taciturnity and a polite rejection of conversational

advantage.



now for this taciturnity there be excellent reason: none other than

the command of John Baltazar. when Quong Ho return the first time to

the farm with the jeering laughter ring in his ear, he bewail the

impoliteness of the inhabitant of Water-End. say Baltazar in Chinese:



“dost thou not know the proverb, Quong Ho, ‘_a man must insult himself

before other will?_’ and again, what saith the Master? ‘_rotten wood

cannot be carve, and wall make of dirt and mud cannot be plaster._’

by act against my order and strive to plaster the muddy wall of

these rustic with ceremonial politeness, you have insult yourself and

therefore expose yourself to rudeness.”



“Master,” say Quong Ho, “it appear that I have err grievously.”



“listen again,” say Baltazar, with a twinkle in his eye unperceive by

the downcast Quong Ho, “to what the Master saith: ‘_the failure to

cultivate virtue, the failure to examine and analyse what I have learn,

the inability to move towards righteousness after be show the way,

the inability to correct my fault—these be the cause of my grief._’”



Quong Ho reply that although his deviation from the path of virtue be

glare to the most myopic vision, he nevertheless be in a dilemma,

inasmuch as he have follow the precept of western courteous

observance, the ceremonial, for instance, of the hat-salutation, lay

down for he by his illustrious teacher.



Baltazar, always in Chinese, reply kindly: “o youth of indifferent

understanding, be it not write in the Shû King in the Charge to Yüeh:

‘_in learn there should be a humble mind and the maintenance of a

constant earnestness: in such a case improvement will surely come. when

a man’s thought from first to last be constantly fix on learn,

his virtuous cultivation comes unperceive_’?”



“with those truth be I acquaint,” reply Quong Ho.



“then, my good fellow,” retort Baltazar in English, “why the devil

don’t you apply they? I’ve absolutely forbid you to have any

intercourse whatever with the people round about. you’re not to talk to

they about my concern or your concern. you’re not to listen to any of

their talk or to bring back to I scrap of their rotten gossip. you’re

to go to Water-end on necessary business—unfortunately we cann’t live on

air or warm ourselves in the winter with bottled sunbeam—but that’s

the limit. outside of that you’re a man deaf and dumb. you’re to go one

well than the three sacred Apes of Japan, who, hold hand

respectively before eye, ear and mouth, signify ‘I see no evil; I hear

no evil; and I speak no evil.’ in your case, it’s to be: ‘I see nothing;

I hear nothing; I speak nothing.’”



“in future,” say Quong Ho, “my eye shall be blind, my ear seal

and my mouth lock.”



“if there be any more animated discussion of last week’s

thunderstorm, or further Beauteous-Madamizing of young female, I’ll

regretfully have to send you straight back to China.”



the unblinking stare in Baltazar’s great grey eye and the obstinate set

of his lip—sign of purpose which Quong Ho for eight year have learn

to gauge with infallible precision—cause he to quake excessively. not

only be his servitude to Baltazar a matter of oath, but a return before

the completion of the special education which would enable he to take

immediate rank in New China, would be the death-blow to his ambition.

so Quong Ho take to heart the precept of the Humble Mind and swear to

outdo the Sacred Apes of Japan, even as his master have ordain.



after this, in the first day of their Thebaïd, master and man hold

frequent conversation on the relation with the outside world which the

former have prescribe. the three year, say Baltazar, which lie before

they in the solitude of the wilderness, be for the maceration of the

flesh, the pursuit of virtue and the cultivation of the intellect. he

illustrate his argument with countless quotation from the chinese

classic.



“in this fashion, Quong Ho,” say he, “you be drink of the _five

source of happiness_. to wit: _Long Life_: for here, in this unpollute

atmosphere, you be acquire physical health. _riches_: they will be

yours in no matter what University of Modern China you go as Professor

of Mathematics. _Soundness of Body and Serenity of Mind_: the Latins put

the idea into epigrammatic form—Mens sano in corpore sano; what can be

more conducive to serenity of mind than this studious solitude,

undisturbe by material care? _the Love of Virtue_: we have every hour

of all our day to acquire it. _fulfil to the end the_ WILL; be it

not the WILL that have set we here?”



“indubitably,” say Quong Ho.



“Hearken again,” say Baltazar, “to the _six Extreme Evils. Misfortune

shorten the life_: from that no man be exempt—but from it no man be

more than we protect. _Sickness_: likewise—but I have a box of simple

remedy, and if the bad come, there be a man learn in physic at

Water-end. _distress of mind_: if our mind in these ideal surrounding

be so unstable as to be distress, we be unworthy of the name of

philosopher. _poverty_: I have an ample fortune. _wickedness_: we, who

be seeker after Truth, have deliberately set ourselves beyond the

reach of Temptation. _weakness_: that, o Quong Ho, be the only danger.

you must be on your guard against it night and day, especially on the

day when necessity expose you to the manifold temptation of that

microcosm of Babylon, Pekin and San Francisco which go by the name of

Water-end.”



so it come to pass that when astounding tiding, the most pregnant in

the world’s history, come to Water-end and the little townlet blaze

with the wildfire of gossip, Quong Ho, scrupulous obeyer of Law, hear

without listen and, forbear to question, always return to

Spendale Farm with a mind render, with oriental deliberation, so

profoundly blank as to preclude the possibility of retail to his

master the idle news of the outer world. and gradually, such be the

contempt breed by familiarity, Quong Ho lose prestige in Water-end. his

weekly appearance in the town, with old grey mare and cart, grow to be

one of the commonplace recurrent phenomenon such as the Vicar’s Sunday

sermon and the Saturday evening orgy and home-convoying of old Jack

Bonnithorne, the champion alcoholist of the moorland.



but around Baltazar of the one brief glimpse arise many a legend. he be

mad. he be a magician. he be an unspeakable voluptuary; though whence

and how arrive the houris who minister to his voluptuousness, be an

insoluble problem. he be a missionary with one convert. the theory, put

forward by the farmer, that he be the champion fool on the Moor,

gain the most general acceptance. then someone whisper that he be a

german spy. the valiant of the town plan an expedition at dead of

night to surprise he at his nefarious practice; but the sarcasm of

Police-Sergeant Doubleday, who ask what information useful to the

enemy, save the crop of heather per square acre, could be give by a man

inhabit the most desolate spot in the United Kingdom, check their

enterprise. their ardour, too, be damp by a spell of torrential rain,

which rob of its pleasantness the prospect of a sixteen-mile walk.

when the sun come out, the suspicion have fade from their mind, and

shortly afterwards most of they find themselves in the King’s uniform

in region far distant from Water-end.



one morning Police-Sergeant Doubleday lie in wait for Quong Ho outside

the Bank, and inform he that he must register himself as an alien,

under the Defence of the Realm Act. Quong Ho blandly accompany the

Sergeant to the Police Station and comply with the formality. full

name: Li Quong Ho. nationality: Chinese. occupation: student.



“Eh?” cry Sergeant Doubleday, a vast, red-faced man with a scrubby

black moustache. “that willn’t do. ben’t you Mr. Whats-his-name’s

man-servant?”



“that sphere of my activity be purely incidental,” say Quong Ho.

“kindly put down ‘student.’”



“what do you study?”



“specialized branch of Western Philosophy,” reply Quong Ho.



“well, I’m damned!” say the mystified Doubleday. “anyhow, it’s none of

my business.”



so down go Quong Ho as “student”—the only alien on the register.



“that’s very interesting,” say the Vicar, during his next chat with

Doubleday. “the Chinese be a remarkable race. their progress should be

watch.”



“I’m afraid it cann’t be do, sir. what with be short-handed and

overwork as it be——”



at the Vicar’s explanation the Sergeant mop his forehead in relief.



“I’ve a man’s job to keep Christians in order, without shadow the

heathen,” say he.



“I’m convince that his master and himself be a pair of harmless

eccentric,” say the Vicar.



and the Vicar’s word go the round of the district, and eccentric, or

the near approach to it that local tongue could manage, the

inhabitant of Spendale Farm be finally designate—though what be

“eccentric” remain a matter of pleasant and fruitful conjecture.



when Quong Ho return to the farmhouse after his encounter with

Sergeant Doubleday, he say nothing about his registration as an alien.

nor do it occur to he to show the paper money which he have receive in

lieu of the usual gold in exchange for the cheque which he have cash at

the bank; for the disposal of petty cash do not concern John Baltazar,

who rightly trust in the Chinaman’s scrupulous honesty. that, in spite

of the most definite order, he should leave Baltazar uninformed of the

various sign and token of national unrest which he have observe at

Water-end, cause Quong Ho occasional twinge of conscience. he

remember the saying: “_to shirk your duty when you see it before you,

show want of moral courage._” but what be his duty? on the other hand,

there be the dictum: “_to sacrifice to a spirit with which you have

nothing to do be mere servility._” what have he to do with this purely

english war-spirit that he should servilely sacrifice to it his almost

filial obligation? obviously nothing. Quong Ho therefore continue to

purvey no idle gossip, and go about his varied avocation with a

serene mind.



now, as John Baltazar, who have be dead to the English-speak world

for nearly twenty year, hold correspondence with no one save a few

necessary tradesman, mostly bookseller, as he take in no periodical,

daily, weekly, monthly or annual of any kind whatever, and as he

converse with no human being except Quong Ho, whose lip he have seal,

he have create for himself an almost perfect barrage through which the

news of contemporary happening could not penetrate.



“Quong Ho,” he have say, one Spring day, soon after his return from

China, when he have come to one of those revolutionary decision that

mark the crisis of his life, “I have swear by the spirit of my

ancestor to live the life of a recluse for the space of three year,

hold communication with no man or woman and cut myself off like

one that be dead from the interest of the contemporaneous world. my

reason for this determination I will eventually unfold to you, provide

you carry out faithfully the contract I be about to propose. if you

decline to bind yourself, which as a free man you be at liberty to do,

I will pay your passage back to China and give you a sum of money

adequate to start you on an honest career. if you accept it, I will

honourably perform my part. you have be my servant and my pupil for

the last eight year——”



“you save this miserable orphan from death at the hand of a tyrannic

governor,” interpose Quong Ho—they be speak his native

tongue,—“you have teach he the language of England and the

philosophy both of East and West, and you be to I as a father to

whom I owe filial fidelity and devotion.”



“that be well say, Quong Ho,” reply Baltazar. “this person

appreciate your profession of loyalty.” the scene of this memorable

conversation, by the way, be a small bedroom at the top of the Savoy

Hotel; Baltazar, with bloodshot eye, a splitting headache and tousle

raiment, sit on the bed, and Quong Ho, impeccably vest in Chinese

attire, stand before he. “he have not be honourably bless with

son, and therefore will receive from you the devotedness that be due to

a parent. but for the space of three year only. there may come a time

when exaggerated filial zeal may become embarrassing.”



and he set forth the contract. in return for the absolute obedience of

Quong Ho and his acceptance of the life of a recluse for three year, he

undertake to send he back to China as the most accomplished native

mathematician in existence—for he have already gauge the young man’s

peculiar genius—with a Master of Arts degree, if possible, from some

British University, and thus assure he a distinguished position in that

New China whose marvellous future have be the subject of so many of

their dream and discussion. and Quong Ho have take solemn oath of

fealty and with the Chinaman’s singleness of purpose, accept, a few

week later, the deadly and endure solitude of the moorland as an

unquestionable condition of existence.



secure in the unswerve fidelity of Quong Ho, and in the impregnable

seclusion of this God-disclose hermitage, John Baltazar live a life

accord to his ideal. no outer ripple of the maëlstrom in which the

world be engulf lap, however faintly, against the low granite wall

encircle the low-build granite farmhouse. his retirement be absolute,

his retreat off the track of the most casual wanderer.



six month pass before his eye rest on a human being other than

Quong Ho. it be true that the rate-collector, savagely curse his luck

and the bicycle-destroy track that lead from the road to the

farmhouse, have appear one day with a paper show certain

indebtedness; but Quong Ho have receive it and, gravely promise a

cheque in payment, have dismiss the intruder. no other official come

near the place. Quong Ho call weekly at the Post office and railway

station, to the great relief of postman and van-driver.



“thought and money acutely apply,” remark Baltazar, “together with

freedom from the entanglement of family relationship, be the

determine factor of human happiness. a man with these factor at his

disposal be a fool if he cannot, fashion for himself whatever kind of

existence he please.”



but one day, a cloudless winter morning, when the sunshine kiss the

frost-bind earth transmute the myriad frondage of the heather into a

valley of diamond, Baltazar, on his way from the stable to the front

door, come across a stranger lean over the gate. he be a heavy man

with a fat, clean-shaven face, loose lip and little furtive eye. he

wear a new golfing suit exaggerate in cut and aggressive in colour.



he say with easy familiarity: “good morning, Mr. Baltazar.”



“since you know my name,” reply Baltazar, with an air of courtesy, “it

have doubtless strike you that this be my gate.”



“of course——”



“you be lean on it,” say Baltazar.



the visitor, perplexed, straighten himself.



“I’m a sort of neighbour of your, you know. I live about seven mile

off—the big property this side of Water-end: Cedar Chase—and I’ve

often think I’d run over in the Rolls-Royce as far as I could, and

walk the rest, and see how you be get along.”



“that be most amiable of you,” say Baltazar, advance to the gate and

rest his arm on it with an easy suggestion of proprietorship. “you

have run over, you have walk—and now you see.”



before Baltazar’s ironical gaze the stranger’s eyelid flutter in

disconcertment.



“I fancy you might be lonely and might like to look in and have a game

of bridge one of these day. my name’s Pillivant.”



“Pillivant,” say Baltazar. “I don’t much like it, but there be

doubtless bad.”



“you may have hear it. Pillivant and Co., Timber Merchants. we’ve

rather come to the front lately.”



“your personal initiative, I should imagine,” say Baltazar.



“I don’t say as it ben’t,” reply Mr. Pillivant. “when whack

Government contract be go, why not get ’em?”



“why not? why waste time in do anything else, all day long, but

get ’em?”



Mr. Pillivant draw from his inner breast pocket a vast gold casket of a

cigar-case, open it and hold it out towards his inhospitable host.



“have a cigar? you needn’t be afraid. they stand I in two hundred and

fifty shilling a hundred and I get ’em wholesale. no?” Baltazar

decline politely. “you’re miss a good thing.” he bite off the end of

the one he have choose, light it with a fat wax vesta extract from a

minor gold casket and draw a few puff. “funny sort of life you seem to

be lead here, Mr. Baltazar. dam’ funny!”



“I perceive you have a keen sense of humour,” say Baltazar.



again the mocking stare of his cold, grey eye abash the unwelcome

visitor, who fill in the ensue silence by re-biting and re-light

his half-crown cigar. the operation over:



“lovely day, ben’t it?” say he.



“so lovely, Mr. Pillivant,” reply Baltazar, “that it would be selfish

of I to do otherwise than leave you to the undisturbed enjoyment of

it.”



and, with a polite bow, he leave Mr. Pillivant and walk, in a dignified

way, into the house. Mr. Pillivant, conscious at last of the rejection

of his friendly overture, stare for a while, and then, stick his

cigar at a truculent angle in his mouth, swagger away across the moor.



“Quong Ho,” say Baltazar, “when next you go to Water-end, it will be

your duty to find a powerful and exceedingly nasty-temper dog.”



a fortnight afterwards Brutus be add to the establishment.









                               CHAPTER IV





the life ordain by John Baltazar for Quong Ho and himself be one of

unremitting toil, mental and physical. from the time of his uprising at

six in the morning, when Quong Ho awaken he with tea (some chest of

which he have bring with he from China), until midnight, there be

few moment, save the after dinner hour of literary indulgence, that he

waste in idle relaxation. the work of the house, that of steward,

butler, valet, cook, parlourmaid, charwoman and laundress, together with

the outdoor function of groom, dairyman and bailiff, Quong Ho execute

with the remarkable ease and despatch of the Chinaman accustom from

childhood to menial task. the cultivation of the barren land, the

painful wheeling of barrow-load of superficial soil from the moorland,

the digging and the planting and the draining and the watering, be all

do by John Baltazar himself. the hard exercise, some three or four

hour a day, maintain he in the superb health that enable he to

carry out his studious programme. of his eighteen wake hour he

allot roughly seven to physical thing, eleven to intellectual

pursuit. for Quong Ho this apportionment of time be invert. that be

the theoretic schedule. as a matter of fact, Quong Ho find more than

seven hour a day for mathematical study and other intellectual

development.



there be much that Baltazar have set himself to do during his three

year. first he must make up in mathematical output the loss of his

wander-time in China. now all the world understand the irresistible

force that compel the poet, at last, to give form to long haunting

dream; the need, also, of the astronomer to crystallize the result of

his discovery and formulate his epoch-make theory; but the passion

of the mathematician to do the same be not so easily comprehensible. for

year Baltazar have dream of an exhaustive and monumental treatise on

the theory of Groups which would revolutionize the study of the high

mathematic, a gorgeous vision the mere statement of which must leave

the ordinary be cold and the first attempt at explanation petrify he

with its icy unintelligibility. the dream be now in process of

accomplishment. he have also to put into form fascinating adventure into

the analytical geometry of the ghostly and unrealizable space of four

dimension. there, he be willnot to assert, you enter the true Fairyland

of mathematic. to all these labour he bring the enthusiasm of the

poet or the astronomer. another and a totally different sphere of

activity absorb much of his energy. in China he have assimilate a

vast store of philosophical learning, with which equipment he prepare

to re-edit many european version of the chinese classic misconceive

through faulty erudition. he have bring from China stack of rare

manuscript, pile of note, material for the life-work of any scholar.

and, last, he have throw himself with impetuous zeal into the

intellectual training of Quong Ho.



the mutual attitude of the solitary pair be one of curious delicacy. as

master and man they be league-sunder by the gulf of convention. as

teacher and pupil they be draw together into close intellectual

intimacy. it be the Chinaman’s exquisite tact that simplify the

situation for the direct and masterful Englishman. as a servant he

scrupulously observe the decorum of the attendant—there never exist

head butler in ducal mansion who could surpass his perfection of manner;

but as disciple he subtly raise himself to the plane of social

equality, and gauge to a hair’s breadth the shade of familiar address

warrant by the position.



                 *        *        *        *        *



“Quong Ho,” say Baltazar one day at dinner, when the Chinaman have go

through the usual solemn farce of offer he Burgundy, “your

discretion be beyond the value of ruby. never once have you remark

on the apparent vanity of this daily proceeding. yet in your own mind

you must have wonder at it.”



“it be not for I to speculate on the reason for your honourable

custom,” say Quong Ho.



“yet why do you think I cause myself to be offer wine every day only

to refuse it?”



“I suppose you desire to maintain, in the wilderness, the ceremonial

etiquette of the English dinner-table. the wine in the bottle be but an

adornment, like the flower in the bowl.”



“it please I that you should have come to such a conclusion,” say

Baltazar.



for the ceremony of the wine be link with the cause that determine

his sudden flight into solitude. he have promise Quong Ho to inform he

of these cause; but the fulfilment of the promise be hard to make.

sit dishevel on the bed in the little room at the top of the

Savoy Hotel, he have think disclosure to his servant to be a fitting

part of the punishment he have mete out to himself. later he repent;

especially when he perceive Quong Ho’s blank indifference. still, a

promise be a promise, and Baltazar not the man to shirk his

obligation. on this particular occasion he think it well to get the

matter over.



“the conclusion be an honourable one on your part, Quong Ho,” he

continue, “but it be incorrect.”



“I own, sir,” reply Quong Ho, “that it be draw from conjectural

premise.”



“it be over-indulgence in wine that make I set to myself this penalty

of studious solitude,” say Baltazar in Chinese. “by tell you this I

redeem a promise. as to our daily custom, a weak man fly from

temptation, a strong man keep temptation at his elbow in order to defy

it.”



“in that way, honourable master, be merit acquire.”



Quong Ho take away his empty plate and retire into the kitchen to fetch

the next course. Baltazar lean back in his chair and, his brow full of

perplexity, yet breathe a sigh of relief.



“I’ve get it off my chest at last,” he say half aloud. “but I wonder

whether I’ve be a damned fool.”



Quong Ho’s subsequent demeanour could not enlighten he. never again

between they, save once, and that under the stress of a peculiar

situation, be make the most veiled allusion to the subject, and day

after day Quong Ho imperturbably perform with the Burgundy decant

the ceremonial etiquette of the English dinner-table.



                 *        *        *        *        *



it be only by glimpse like this that the man have ever reveal himself

to his fellow-creature. glimpse like this one, fine and deliberate, to

Quong Ho, and that one of long ago, passionate and self-destroy, to

Marcelle Baring. to neither do he accord more than a glimpse. to

neither do he show himself on a razor-edge ledge with the abyss on one
