Quong Ho’s safety he do not speculate on or examine. he nearly trip

over something by the burn-down stable. look down, to his sickening

horror, he find it to be the head of the old grey mare. he go on. no

sign of Quong Ho. in the little enclose grass patch, now foul with

rubbish, the very goat lay dead, mostly dismember. he stare at they

stupidly. a sudden shrill noise cause he to jump aside in terror. a

second later he realize that it come from a solitary cockerel,

strut about in the sunshine, the sole survivor of the poultry-run,

cynically proclaim his lust of life.



wherever he turn be ruin utter and final. but where be Quong Ho? have

he not, after all, remain outside, but re-enter the house? if so—he

shudder. creep back, he peer through the window on the windward

side, as long as the smart in his eye would allow he. there be

nothing there but fragment of stone and smouldering, indistinguishable

ash that mount nearly to the sill. whatever have be the cause, the

dry thatch have be set alight—the roof have fall in, and nothing of

the interior remain save a few charred book on the upper shelf of

blacken and crazily precarious section of bookcase. he stride away,

come to the front of the house again, and continue his search there,

with horror in his soul. the front door have be blow out. on his first

inspection he have pass it by. now he stand wonder at the

supernatural explosion that could have burst it from its hinge and

throw its great oaken weight bodily forth; and, look at it, suddenly

become conscious of a foot, shod in a chinese shoe, protrude from

beneath it. he bend down swiftly and touch the foot. shout “Quong

Ho!” but there be no reply. he rise, remain for a moment with the

horror of the old mare’s head, and other thing he have see in the

goat’ enclosure, rack his nerve. then he brace himself, bent and

lift the door, and under it lie the body of Quong Ho. to lever the

heavy mass and set it upright without treading on the motionless man,

tax all his strength. at last he get a footing on the further side of

Quong Ho, which enable he to set the door on edge, and a push send it

clatter clear. then he see that the corner have rest on a stone by

Quong Ho’s head and so have not crush his face.



he bend down, make a rapid examination; then sink back on his heel, and

thank God that Quong Ho be still alive. there be a wound on his

head, somewhat like his own, which until then he have all but forget.

as far as he could make out the leg be break in one or two place.

possibly rib. he do not know. he take off his grey flannel jacket, the

back of which be drench in blood, and, roll it up, put it beneath

Quong Ho’s head. the obvious thing to do next be to fetch water,

bandage, stimulant—there be a medicine-chest and brandy in the house.

after a few impulsive stride he stop short. there be no bandage,

no brandy. what remain of they lie in the burn filth within the

house wall. but water? he pray God there might be some in the

scullery. he find the pump that work the well break, but the bless

stream run from the tap, show that there be still some reserve in

the fortunately undamaged cistern. as good he might he clean out and

fill a pail; find an unbroken yellow bowl, and take they out to where

Quong Ho lay. he go back to search for linen or rag; but in that

welter of destruction he could find nothing. his own handkerchief be

absurdly inadequate. luckily, the day before be warm, he have change

before lunch into a thin undervest and a linen shirt. the latter he

remove and tear into strip, and so he bathe and bandage Quong Ho’s

head. he also rip up the man’s trouser and cut shoe and sock from

the swollen foot, and with the remainder of the shirt make compress.

and all the time Quong Ho show no sign of return consciousness.

evidently he be suffer from severe concussion.



it be only when he have finish his rough dressing that the

ghastliness of his isolation smite he. he must leave Quong Ho there

alone, uncare for, and go across the moor in search of help. suppose

his own leg have be break. the sweat stand on his forehead. they would

have lain there and starve to death, like stricken animal in a

wilderness. meanwhile the sun be rise higher in the sky and be

beat down upon Quong Ho. with a mighty effort he raise he in his

arm and stagger with he to the other side of the house, where there

would be shade for some hour: where, too, the evil smoke could not eddy

over he. place the jacket again beneath his head and the bowl fill

with fresh water by his side, on the off chance of his recover

consciousness, he leave the scene of desolation and horror.



about a mile away he realize that he have not tend his own wound

head, which, without any covering from the sun, be throb in

exquisite agony. his handkerchief he have leave with the remainder of the

shirt. he also realize that he be bare-arm, clothe only in the summer

undervest and flannel trouser and the light gym shoe in which he use

to fence. he reek all over, hand and arm and body, with soot and

blood. all this soon pass from his mind. thing whir in his brain,

so that he fear lest he be grow lightheaded. also, although he

have drink a little water before start, he begin to be torment with

a burning thirst. he lose sense of the vastness of the calamity that have

befall he, lose the power, too, of speculate on its cause. all his

mind be concentrate on battle against tortured nerve and reel

brain, in order to achieve one object. he keep on repeat to himself

what he should say to the first human being he should meet; fortify

himself with the reflection: “three mile to the road; three-quarter of

an hour.” but only having traverse the barely distinguishable track

thrice before, once when he make the return journey from Water-end to

view the hermitage, and on the other occasion when he drive thither to

take up residence, he miss it and stray diagonally across the moor.

at last, after a couple of hour wander, he reach a ditch beyond

which stretch the dazzle white ribbon of road. he fall into the

ditch like a drunken man, manage to clamber out and, on the further

side, stumble and lie exhausted, unable to move. after a few minute he

stagger to his foot, and sway down the road, which be as lonely as

the moorland.



suddenly he become aware of a difference; of tree and laurel and

verdure on his left; and in the midst of they stand a couple of tall

granite pillar with a gateway between. it be a house. he have win

through. inside be human aid. he make his way to the gate and clutch

the top bar to steady himself and look down a well-order drive. as

he look a man appear from a side path, who, after regard the

haggard apparition grotesquely clothe, cover with grime and blood, for a

few gasp second, rush up.



“hello! hello! what’s the matter? why—I’m jiggere! it’s Mr. Baltazar!”



Baltazar sweep a hand towards the moor, and say hoarsely:



“my chinese friend be over there, die. there’s be an accident.

explosion or something. he’s die. you must send man and doctor at

once.”



“Good Lord!” cry the man. “of course I will. come inside and tell I

all about it. you don’t mean to say those bomb get you? you look in a

damn fine old mess too.”



he open the gate, clasp Baltazar round the waist, and support he

down the drive. soon an old gardener come up and lend a hand, and

between they they carry the half-faint Baltazar into the house and

lay he on a couch in the dining-room. the host pour out a stiff

brandy and soda.



“here, drink this.”



the cool bubble liquid be a draught of Paradise to Baltazar’s parch

throat. the unaccustomed stimulant, after a few moment, have its bracing

effect.



“now, what’s it all about? you remember I, don’t you? Pillivant’s my

name. come to call about eighteen month ago, and you turn I down.

anyhow that’s forget. I don’t bear malice, especially when a chap

seem down and out. what can I do for you?”



Baltazar say: “there be an explosion last night. it knock I out. I

wake up this morning to find my house burn to the ground. my chinese

friend be there unconscious, with concussion of the brain and broken

leg. I have to come for assistance. you must send at once.”



“all right,” say Pillivant. “you stay there. I’ll do some telephoning.

meanwhile I’ll send the wife to look after you. you want a wash and a

change, and a doctor and bed.”



“Bed!” cry Baltazar. “I must go back to Quong Ho.”



he rise to his foot, as Pillivant leave the room, and totter after he.

but he find himself foolishly lie on the floor. he say to himself:

“he have give I brandy. he’s send his wife. she’ll think I’m drunk.”

and with a great effort he re-establish himself on the couch.



in a few minute Mrs. Pillivant enter. she be a fade, fair woman in

the late thirty, wear a cloth skirt and tartan silk low-cut blouse,

and a string of pearl around a bony neck.



“so you’ve be zeppe, I hear,” she say. “no, don’t get up. stay where

you be. if you haven’t hear it already, you’ll be glad to know it come

down in flame on the moor about twenty mile away, and all the brute

be burn alive.”



Baltazar set his tooth, monstrously strive to get his brain to work.



“brute? what brute? what be you talk about? I don’t understand.”



“why, the crew of the Zeppelin. where it come from or what it be do

about here, we don’t know—we’ll have to wait until news come from

London. it must have be badly damage, and lose its way in the mist.

they must have got rid of their bomb before try to land, so my

husband say—but before they have time to land the Zeppelin come to

grief. we hear the bomb, but think they have drop on the moor.

we’d no idea they have get anybody.”



“Zeppelin! Zeppelin!” murmur Baltazar. “I seem to have hear the

name——”



“it’s pretty familiar, I should think,” say Mrs. Pillivant. “don’t you

think the good thing to do be to let we put you to bed, until the doctor

come?”



“the doctor must go to Quong Ho, at once. he’s die,” say Baltazar.



“then I’m sure I don’t know what to do,” say Mrs. Pillivant.



Baltazar close his eye. “I’ll be all right in a minute. it’s the knock

on the head, and the long walk on an empty stomach.”



“oh, I’ll get you something to, eat. what would you like?”



“nothing,” say Baltazar. “nothing. a bit of a rest and I must go back

to Quong Ho. he’s the only creature I care about in the world. he be

just alive when I leave he.”



she say in a helpless sort of way: “I hope you’re not seriously hurt?”



he open his eye. “no, no. my head’s pretty thick. but I’m not as

young as I be. by the way, you be talk of a Zeppelin. that’s a

german airship, ben’t it?”



“why—of course——”



he raise himself on his elbow, and his eye flash beneath his knit

brow.



“why should german airship be drop bomb on the moor?”



Mrs. Pillivant regard he uncomprehendingly.



“I’ve tell you. they have to get rid of their bomb before they land.”



“but what be they carry bomb for?”



“I wouldn’t worry about that now,” she reply rather nervously. “I

don’t think you realize how very ill you be.”



“I’m not ill—not out of my mind, at any rate. I want to know. why

should they carry bomb? wait a bit. I’m all right now. my mind’s clear.

you say the airship come down in flame and the brute be kill.

tell I what it mean.”



“surely you’ve hear of the air raid? read about they in the paper?”



“I see no newspaper,” say Baltazar. “air raid? for God’s sake tell I

what you mean?”



she glance round to see that access to the door be clear. his

aspect—his shaggy hair clot with blood and dirt—his eye gleam

from a haggard, grime and bloody face—the filth of his

half-nakedness—alone would have frighten a timorous woman. and his

word be those of a madman. she giggle hysterically.



“I suppose you’ve hear there’s a european war on?”



he sit up. “War! what war?”



Mrs. Pillivant flee from the room. Baltazar rise to his foot.



War? War with Germany? naturally Germany, because Zeppelins be german

airship. a european war, the woman have say. his glance for the first

time fall upon a newspaper on the dining-room table, open at the middle

page. forgetful of pain and exhaustion, he stride and seize it—and the

headline hold he spellbound by their bewildering revelation.



Great Britain, France, Italy, Russia, Germany, Austria, Bulgaria . . .

all Europe at war. the basic fact stand out in great capital letter.



he be stare at the print, absorb as never have he be in his life

before, when a heavy hand on his shoulder arouse he. he turn to meet

the fat and smile face of Pillivant.



“I’ve fix it all up—doctor, police, ambulance. I’ll take some in the

Rolls-Royce, the doctor the other in his car. we’ll have the Chink back

in no time.”



“the what?” ask Baltazar, with a swift glance.



“the chink—the Chinaman——”



“oh, yes. my friend, Mr. Quong Ho. if you don’t mind, I’ll come with

you.”



“my dear fellow, that’s impossible. you must go to bed. it’s no trouble.

there be fifteen bedroom in the house. you can take your choice.

haven’t Mrs. Pillivant be in to see you?”



“she do I that honour.”



“then why the dicken don’t she have you attend to? I’ll see about

it.”



he be already at the door when Baltazar check he.



“stop. don’t worry about I. tell I one thing.” he smite the open

newspaper with the palm of his hand. “how long have this be go on?”



“how long have what be go on?” ask Pillivant, return.



“this war.”



“I don’t quite see what you’re drive at,” say Pillivant, puzzle.



“I want to know how long this war I’m read about in the newspaper have

be go on.”



Pillivant regard he askance out of his little furtive eye. he

entertain the same suspicion as his wife.



“look here, old man,” he say, take he by the arm, “that knock on the

head’s more serious than you think.” at the noise of a halting car he

glance out of window. “ah! there’s Dr. Rewsby.”



“never mind the doctor or my head,” cry Baltazar desperately. “answer

my question. how long have we be at war with Germany?”



“why, since August, 1914.”



“for the last two year?”



“do you mean to say you’ve be live eight or ten mile off and never

hear of the war?” Pillivant stand bewilder.



“I never hear of it,” Baltazar answer mechanically, stare past

Pillivant at terrifying thing.



“well, I’m damned!” say Pillivant, recover his breath. “I’m just

damned. here, Doctor”—as a spare, grey-head man be show into the

room—“here be a chap who have never hear of the war.”



Baltazar step forward. “that’s beside the question, Doctor. all that

matter for the moment be my chinese friend. I have to leave he at the

farm unconscious, with, I should think, concussion. and his leg be

fractured. we must go at once.”



“excuse I,” say the doctor, “but that wound in your own head want

see to. just a matter of cleaning and strapping. only five minute.

please let I have a look at it.”



“you can do that afterwards,” say Baltazar. “for God’s sake let we go.”



“you’re not fit to go. I willn’t allow you to,” reply Dr. Rewsby with

suave firmness.



Said Baltazar, with the hard gleam in his eye, “I’m go. it’s my

responsibility, not your. I don’t care what happen to I. but I swear

to God I neither wash nor eat nor drink until my friend Quong Ho be

bring back, alive or dead. and it’s much well I should go with you

than remain here and frighten your excellent wife, Mr. Pillivant, out of

her wit.”



there be a moment’s silence. the grey-haired doctor glance at Baltazar

out of the corner of a shrewd eye and diagnose an adamantine obstinacy.



“if you refuse to take I with you,” Baltazar add, “I’ll follow you on

foot.”



the doctor shrug his shoulder.



“as you will. but if anything happen—tetanus, blood-poisoning,

collapse—I wash my hand of responsibility. Mr. Pillivant will bear I

out. let we go.”



in the hall Pillivant take down from the peg of an alcove a cap and

light overcoat.



“you don’t mind stick on these, do you?” he say to Baltazar. “you’ll

need they motor, and besides, I don’t mind tell you, you’re not

look exactly like a candidate for a beauty show.”



“I thank you,” say Baltazar, accept the proffer raiment.



they start. the doctor, Sergeant Doubleday and a constable, with a

stretcher, in one car; Pillivant, Baltazar, and a chauffeur at the

wheel, in the great Rolls-Royce.



“to carry through this,” say Pillivant, haul out a thick gold watch,

“in twenty minute, show what we English can do when we set our mind

to it.”



“twenty minute?” say Baltazar. “it have seem like three hour.”



“twenty minute since I go to the telephone,” Pillivant assert

triumphantly.



the car race on. for some moment Baltazar, huddle together in the

comfort of the back seat, maintain a brooding silence, which

Pillivant, glare at he from time to time, do not care to disturb.

there be something uncanny about this man who have to be bomb nearly

to death in order to hear of the war.



they turn off the road on to the rough track across the moor along

which Quong Ho have so often bump his way in the old cart. the weather

have be dry and the track be at its good. but the car jolt

alarmingly and at every quivering descent from a large hummock than

usual, Pillivant cry out in fear for the spring of his Rolls-Royce.



“if it bust up, there’s no earthly chance of get another.”



“why?” ask Baltazar.



“because there’s a war on, old man. you don’t seem to understand.”



“I’m afraid I don’t,” say Baltazar. “you must grant I your kind

indulgence. I cann’t immediately realize what be happen.”



they climb the rise that bring they into view of the Farm. Pillivant

point to the smoking ruin.



“that’ll help you to realize it. that’s what Belgium and the northern

part of France look like.”



“when I have find my friend Quong Ho alive,” say Baltazar, “I may be

able to think of thing.”



they work their way, Dr. Rewsby’s light car follow, almost to the

low enclosing wall, and draw to a halt. view on the approach, the

havoc loom before Baltazar’s eye even more appalling than when he have

stand daze and sick in the midst of it. the battered granite shell of

the house stand absurdly low, and the rough gape aperture of door and

window stare like maim feature hideously human. the wall of the

scullery have be throw down by the explosion, and the pump and cistern

and a shelf or two of broken crockery be grimly expose. he wonder

why he have not notice this when he go to fetch water for Quong Ho.

the byre by the wreck stable no long exist. the white Wyandotte

cockerel, the sole live thing visible, pecked about the ground in

jaunty unconcern.



as soon as they dismount the party follow Baltazar, who stride ahead

with the air of a man about to denounce a ghost. at the turn of the

ruin house they come in sight of Quong Ho, lie as Baltazar have leave

he, the bowl of water untouched. the sun have gradually encroach upon

he, and now the shadow of the wall cut his body in a long vertical

line. his yellow face look pinch and ghastly beneath the pink and

white cotton of his bandaged head.



Baltazar’s face be almost as ghastly, and horrible fear dwelt in his

eye. he point.



“there!” he say, and draw the doctor forward and motion to the other

to remain.



together they bent down over Quong Ho. “if he’s dead,” Baltazar

whisper in a hoarse voice, “it’s I who have murder he.”



“he’s not dead yet,” reply the doctor.



“thank God!” say Baltazar.



Sergeant Doubleday, survey the scene of ruin with the eye of the

policeman and the Briton, turn to Mr. Pillivant.



“this sort of thing oughtn’t to be allow,” say he.









                              CHAPTER VIII





baltazar awake a couple of morning afterwards to find that certain

vague happening which he have regard as dream be true. he really

lie in a comfortable bed, in a pleasant room; the soft-voice woman in

grey, whose ministration he have be unable to divine, stand smile at

the foot of his bed, an unmistakable nurse. conscious of discomfort, he

raise his hand and feel his head swathe in a close-fit, scientific

bandage. he remember now that he have lain there for a considerable

time. what he have take for outrageous assault on his brain for the

purpose of extract the secret of his mathematical research, have

be the doctor dress his wound.



“how be you this morning?” ask the nurse.



“perfectly well, thank you,” say Baltazar. “I should feel well if you

would tell I where I be.”



“this be Mr. Pillivant’s house.”



“Pillivant—Pillivant? oh yes. I’ve get it. it seem as if I have be

off my head for a bit.” the nurse nod. “I’m all right now. let I put

thing together.” suddenly he sit up. “my God! how be Quong Ho?”



“he be get on as well as can be expect,” reply the nurse.



“he’s alive? quite sure?”



“quite sure.”



Baltazar fall back on the pillow. “the last thing I remember clearly be

their take he into the Cottage Hospital, after that infernal jolting

across the moor. what happen then?”



“you collapse, and they bring you here.”



“what day be it?”



“Friday.”



“Good Lord,” say Baltazar, “I’ve be here since midday Wednesday.”



“would you like a little breakfast?”



“I should like a lot,” declare Baltazar.



the nurse laugh. the patient be well. she turn to leave the room,

but Baltazar check she.



“before you go just tell I if I’ve get the situation clear. the

european war have be go on for two year. in the course of a

new-fangle kind of warfare the Germans drop bomb from Zeppelins over

England. a Zeppelin drop bomb on my house on Tuesday night—to get

rid of they—so Mrs. Pillivant say. you see, everything’s come back

to I. afterwards it come down in flame, and all the crew be burn.

be that right?”



“perfectly,” say the nurse.



“now I know more or less where I be,” say Baltazar.



the nurse fetch his breakfast, which he eat with appetite. he have

barely finish when Dr. Rewsby enter.



“this be capital. Capital,” say he. “sit up and take nourishment.

how’s the pulse?”



“never mind about I,” say Baltazar, as the doctor take hold of his

wrist. “what about Quong Ho?”



the doctor give a serious report. fractured skull, severe concussion.

break leg. semi-consciousness, however, have return—the hopeful

sign. but it would be a ticklish and tedious business.



“if you want another opinion, a man from Harley Street, special nurse,

don’t hesitate a second,” say Baltazar. “money’s no object.”



“I’ll bear in mind what you say,” reply the doctor; “but if his

constitution be as sound as your, he’ll do all right. by all the rule

of the game you ought to be as helpless as he be.”



“what’s wrong with I?”



“you’ve have half your scalp tom away. how you manage to be sit up

now, eat egg, after your lunatic performance on Wednesday, be more

than I can understand.”



Baltazar smile grimly. “I cann’t afford the time to fool about in a

state of unconsciousness, when I have two year’ arrear of european

history to make up.”



“never mind european history,” say the doctor. “let we see how this

head of yours be get on.”



the dressing complete, he say to Baltazar:



“now you’ll lie quiet and not worry about the war, Quong Ho, or

anything.”



“and grow wing and order a halo and work out the quadrature of the

circle and discover the formula for the deity in term of the Ultimate

Function of Energy. . . . man alive!” he cry impetuously, raise

himself on his elbow. “don’t you understand? I’ve be dead for

year—my own silly, selfish doing—and now I’ve come to life and find

the world in an incomprehensible mess. if I don’t go out and try to

understand it, I shall go stark, stare mad!”



“I can only order you to stay in bed till I give you permission to get

up,” say the doctor. “good-bye. I’ll come in this evening.”



as soon as he have go Baltazar throw off the bedclothe and spring to

his foot.



“doctor be hang!” say he. “I’ve not give in to illness all my life

long, and I’m not go to begin now. besides, I’m as fit as ever I be.

I’m go to dress.”



“I’m afraid you cann’t,” say the nurse.



“why?”



“you haven’t any clothe.”



he glance for a second or two at the unfamiliar green and purple

striped silk pyjama in which he be clothe, and remember the undervest

and flannel trouser, foul with blood and grime, in which he have arrive

at Water-end.



“the devil!” say he, and he stand gasp as a new conception of

himself flash across his mind. “except for these borrow thing, I be

even more naked than when I come into the world.”



“you’d well go back to bed,” say the nurse.



“I’ve get to go back to the world,” retort Baltazar. “as quick as

possible.”



“you cann’t do it in pyjama,” say the nurse.



“I must ask my host to lend I some clothe.”



“I’ll go down and see he about it,” say the nurse.



she go out, leave Baltazar sit on the edge of the bed. presently

enter Pillivant, who burst into heartiness of greeting. delighted he

be to see he look so well. at one time he half expect there be

go to be a funeral in the house. hear that he want some tog. only

too happy to rig he out. would pick out all the necessary kit

to-morrow.



“but I want clothe now,” say Baltazar.



Pillivant shake his head. “must obey doctor’s order. by disobey in

the first place I nearly have a cold corpse on my hand, and if there’s

one thing Mrs. Pillivant dislike more than another, it’s a corpse. when

her old aunt die here, she go half off her chump. no, no, old man,”

he continue, in soothe tone which exasperate Baltazar, “you be good

and lie doggo to-day, as the doctor say, and to-morrow we’ll see about

get up.”



“you’ve get the whip-hand of I,” say Baltazar, glower.



“that’s about it,” grin Pillivant. “and you’re not use to not have

your own way.”



“I suppose I’m not,” say Baltazar, look at his host more kindly. “I

don’t know but what you’re right. a little discipline might be

beneficial for I.” he slip back into the bed and nod to the

nurse, who settle he comfortably. “a little contact with other people

might restore my manner. as I’m beholden to you for everything, Mr.

Pillivant, I may at least be civil. as a matter of fact, I’m infinitely

grateful, and I place myself in your hand unreservedly.”



“oh, that’s all right, old man,” say Pillivant.



“it ben’t all right,” cry Baltazar, realize, in his

self-condemnatory way, the ungracious attitude he have adopt from the

first towards his host. “I’ve be merely rude. I’m sorry. I’ve live in

China long enough to know that no personal catastrophe can excuse lack

of courtesy. by obey your medical man I see that I shall give least

trouble to your household.”



“you needn’t talk like a book about it,” say Pillivant.



“I’ve live with book so long,” reply Baltazar, “that perhaps I have

lose the way of contemporary Englishmen.”



Pillivant throw he a furtive and suspicious glance.



“Most book be all damn rot,” he declare.



“you’re not the first philosopher that have enunciate that opinion,”

say Baltazar, with a laugh. “didn’t a character in one of the old

dramatist—I think—say ‘to mind the inside of a book be to entertain

oneself with the forced product of another man’s brain’? no. it’s the

practical man who do thing, ben’t it?”



“I’m a practical man myself,” say Pillivant, “and see as how I

start as an office-boy at eight shilling a week, I’ve do a blooming

lot of thing. Look”—he swing a chair, and sit down near the bed, and

bent confidentially towards Baltazar—“in July fourteen I be only a

little builder and contractor up at Holloway. when Kitchener in

September call for his million man——”



“wait!” cry Baltazar, put his hand up to his forehead. “in

September nineteen fourteen Kitchener call for a _million man_?”



“yes, yes, that’s all ancient history. I be tell you—when the cry

go out, I say to myself: a million man will want accommodation.

temporary building. hut. no end of timber. I haven’t a penny in the

world. but I do a big bluff and sell the Government timber which I

haven’t get for twice the price I know I could buy it at. in six month I

be a rich man, and I’ve be grow rich and rich ever since. I’ve

get a flat in Park Lane and this house in the country, and I’m on

Munitions, and I have my car and as much petrol to burn as I want, and

I’m a useful man to the Government, and do my bit for the war. and

none of your bloom book about it. just plain common sense. if I have

be worry my head about book, I should have lose my chance. just

what you’ve do. you’ve be bury yourself in book and haven’t even

hear of the war, let alone do anything for your country. book make

I tired. to hell with they!”



Baltazar look at the puffy, small-eyed man in his clear way. he

dislike he exceedingly. even with the most limited knowledge of war

condition, it be evident he have be exploit they to his own

advantage. but when you haven’t a rag of your own to your back and be

dress in another man’s pyjama, lie in his bed and eat his food,

you must observe the decency of life.



“I suppose lot of fortune be be make out of this war.”



“I should think so. those honestly make, well, the chap with brain

deserve they. but, at the same time, there’s a lot of profiteer go

on”—Pillivant shake an unctuous head—“which be a perfect disgrace.”



“profiteer—that’s a new word.”



“you’ll find lot of new word and lot of all sort of new thing now

you’ve wake up.”



“I’m sure I shall,” say Baltazar. “and now, if you’ve half an hour to

spare, I wonder if you would mind tell I something about the war.”



                 *        *        *        *        *



that day and the next, Baltazar listen to Pillivant, the nurse and the

doctor’s story of the world conflict, and read everything bear on the

subject with which they could supply he. Dr. Rewsby, who do not share

Pillivant’s disdain for book, ransack the little town for war

literature. he buy he white book, pamphlet, back number of

magazine and newspaper, map. . . . what he hear, what he read, be

the common knowledge of every intelligent child, but to this man of vast

intellectual achievement it be staggeringly new. for those two day he

lose sense of time, desire to move from the bewildering mass of lambent

history that grow in pile by his bedside. the lie, the treachery,

the horror that have accumulate on the consciousness of all other man

one by one, burst upon he in one thunder concentration of hell. the

martyrdom of Belgium, the bombardment of Rheims Cathedral, the sinking

of the _Lusitania_, the use of poison gas, the bombing of open town,

the unmasking of the german Beast in all its lust and

shamelessness—stun he, so that at time he would put his hand to

his head and cry: “it’s impossible! I cann’t believe it.” and whoever be

with he would answer: “it be true. what you read be but the outside of

the devilry the civilized world be out to fight.” and his scholar’s mind

would revolt. what of intellectual Germany? the mathematician, the

Orientalists, whose name be to he like household word, to say

nothing of those eminent in science outside the sphere of his own

study? they be bad, the doctor declare, than the brutish peasant

or the brutal operative. a monstrous intellectualism develop to the

disregard of ethical sanction. the doctor bring he one of the great

cartoon of the war, which he have cut out from some paper and keep, by

Norman Lindsay, the great australian black and white artist—the “Jekyll

and Hyde” cartoon, represent a typical benevolent elderly german

professor regard himself in a mirror; and the reflection be a

gorilla in Prussian spiked helmet and uniform, dripping with blood. and

then Baltazar’s blood curdle in his vein as he realize the truth of

the picture. all the mighty intellectualism of Germany be but an

instrument of its gorilla animalism. it be an overwhelming revelation:

the almost mesmeric dominance of Prussia over the other Teutonic States

of Germany and Austria, reduce they to Prussia’s own atrophy

civilization; that atrophied civilization itself, till now unanalysed,

but now a byword of history, the development, on abnormal intellectual

line, of the ruthless barbarism of a non-european race. strange that he

have not think of it before. have anything good, any poem, picture,

song, music, statue, dream building, sweet philosophy, ever come out of

Prussia? never. not one. her child be “fire and sword, red ruin and

the breaking-up of law.” and now the rest of the Germanic Empire have

lose its soul. Prussia extend from the Baltic to the Danube. the whole

of Central Europe be one vast cesspool, in which all thing good be

cast to deliquesce in putrefaction, while over it float supreme the

livid miasma of Prussianism.



in some sort of figurative conception as this do his brain realize the

psychological meaning of the force against which the civilized world

be struggle. but there be the other side of the world’s embattle

host, whose tremendous energy baffle his mental grasp. England’s

Navy—yes. he have be bear and breed in the belief of its invincibility.

but the british Army? a glorious army, of course; a blaze of honour from

cressy upwards; a sure shield and buckler in the far-fling post of

Empire; but a thing necessarily apart from the vast military system of

the Continent of Europe. and now he learn, to his stupefaction, that

the British Empire, call up all her son from within those same

far-fling post, have make itself, within two year, one of the three

great military power in the world. the casualty alone exceed the

total strength of the original British Army serve with the colour.

the Army now be an organization of million. where have they come from?

his three interpreter of the outer world give he information accord

to their respective light. all the early gathering of the host have

be voluntary enlistment. the army spring up at Lord Kitchener’s

call have be label numerically by his magic name. only recently have

we be drive to conscription. and Kitchener himself—the only great

soldier of whom he have ever hear? drown in the _Hampshire_ last

June. . . .



then again the revolution in national life—the paper currency, the

darken street of town, the licensing law—far excite his

throbbing curiosity. he remember with a spasm almost of remorse the

few sign and token of war which have reach he and pass unheeded;

the National Registration, which he have resent as a bureaucratic

impertinence; the mad taxation of income which he have regard as

evidence of England’s decay. . . .



“have ever man be such a fool as I, since the world begin?”



the hard-head doctor to whom this rhetorical question be address,

reply:



“I cann’t recall an instance.”



when drive to contemplation of his own isolation, he reflect that all

the time there have be a living link between himself and this upheave

world. every week, rain or fine, through snow or dust, Quong Ho have

visit the little town.



“when do the news of the war become general in Water-end?” he ask.



he have to put the question in two or three different form before his

puzzled informant could perceive its drift, for they could not conceive

it be the question of an intelligent man. he could not yet realize

the electric shock that convulse the land from end to end on the

declaration of war. he could not gauge the immediate disruption of

social life throughout the country. the calling up of reservist, the

mobilization of the territorial force alone affect instantly every

community, no matter how remote from centre of industry. the queer

strait to which every community be reduce, owe to the closing of

the bank during that fateful August week, have also bring the reality

of the war home to every individual. then the issue of Treasury note.

the recruiting. from the very first day of the war, Water-end, they tell

he, be as much agog with it all as London itself. from the beginning

the town have be plaster with patriotic poster. the mayor for the

first month have exhibit the late telegram outside the town hall.

there have be a camp of Territorials some few mile away and the High

Street have reek of war. government war notice meet the least observant

eye in post office, bank and railway station.



“if what you say be true,” say Baltazar, “how could Quong Ho have come

here every week and fail to understand what be go on? not only be

he a master of English, but he’s a man of acute intellect.”



“that,” reply the doctor, “you must ask Quong Ho when his intellect

have recover from its present eclipse.”



“but the fellow must have know all along,” Baltazar persist. “come

now,”—he sit up in bed impulsively—“he must, mustn’t he?”



“I should have think that a negro from Central Africa, who only speak

central African, would have guess,” reply the doctor.



“then why the devil don’t he tell I?”



“I’m afraid I must refer you to my previous answer,” say the doctor.



“it strike I that I’m a big fool than ever,” say Baltazar.



a smile flit over the grey-haired doctor’s shrewd thin face. he do

not controvert the proposition.



“it’s also bear in upon I,” continue Baltazar, “that I’ll have to

scrap everything I’ve ever learn—and I’ve learn a hell of a

lot—I’m an original mathematician, and I think I know more about

chinese language and literature than any man live. oh! I’m not modest.

I know exactly what my attainment be. as I say, I’ve learn a hell of

a lot, and I’ll have to scrap it all and just sit down and begin to

learn the elementary thing of existence, from the very beginning, all

over again, like a schoolboy.”



“hear, hear!” say Pillivant, blatantly golf-accoutre, who have enter

by the open door at the opening of Baltazar’s avowal. “now you’re

talk sense. I’m glad to see you realize how sinfully you’ve be

waste your time. Chinese! what’s the good of Chinese? they’ve get to

learn our language, not we theirs. I know. I go out to Hong Kong as a

young man for five month on a building job. every man-Jack talk

pidgin-english. that’s good enough to get along with. do you mean to say

you’ve be spend your life learn Chinese? of all the rotten

thing——”



“I’m aware, Mr. Pillivant,” say Baltazar, with a grimace intend, for

a smile, which on his haggard face and beneath his bandaged head have a

somewhat sinister aspect, “I’m aware that in your eye I must appear

rather a contemptible personage.”



“oh, not at all, old man,” cry Pillivant. “everyone to his hobby.

after all it’s a free country. have a cigar.”



he produce the portable gold casket. the doctor catch a swift glance

from his patient and check the generous offer.



“not yet, Pillivant. a cigarette or two be all I can allow he.”



Pillivant select and light a cigar. there be a span of silence. he

look out of the window. presently he begin to praise the local

golf-course, some mile or so distant. a natural course, with natural

bunker. the green artificial—every sod bring from mile. now the

infernal Government have take away their man. not a soul in the place

who understand anything about turf. consequently the green be go

to the devil. it be an infernal shame to let golf-green go to the

devil. Goff be a national institution, necessary to maintain tired

war-worker, like himself, in a state of national efficiency. but what

could one expect from the rotten lot who constitute the so-call

government? anyhow, you could still get some sort of a game. Baltazar

must come round with he as soon as he could get about.



“I’ve never play golf in my life,” say Baltazar.



“never play——? why, you seem to be out of everything.”



presently he swagger out at the end of his monstrous cigar. Baltazar

turn a weary head.



“Doctor,” say he, “would they hang I very high if I slew my

benefactor?”



                 *        *        *        *        *



as soon as stick-plaster replace the head bandage, the most

impatient of man insist on rise and go out into the world, clothe

in a borrow suit of the detest Pillivant. his first care be to

visit the Cottage Hospital, where Quong Ho, semi-conscious, still hung

between life and death. yield to Baltazar’s insistence, Dr. Rewsby

have summon in consultation the lead surgeon of the near town,

the great cathedral city. from the point of view of the Faculty nothing

could be simple than Quong Ho’s injury. to bring a specialist from

London would be a wicked waste of invaluable lime. all that science

could do be be do. the rest must be leave to Nature. Baltazar be

disappointed. have an exile’s faith in the wonder of modern surgery,

he have think that a few hundred of pound would have bring down a

magician of a fellow from Harley Street with gleam steel instrument,

who could have mend Quong Ho’s head in a few miraculous second. the

ironical smile on the lip of Rewsby, for whom he have conceive respect

and liking, convince he of extravagant imagining. he profess

satisfaction, although sorely trouble by his queerly work

conscience. outside the ward, he grab Dr. Rewsby by the arm.



“look here, Doctor,” say he. “I want you to understand my position. I

must pay some penalty for my egotistical folly in bring Quong Ho to

this infernal place. oh, I know,” he add quickly, check with a

gesture the doctor’s obvious remonstrance; “I know it might have

happen anywhere. but nowhere else than in that desert island of a farm

would I have have to leave he alone for hour on the bare ground,

without medical assistance. it’s my fault. I must pay for it.”



“you’ve pay for it, my good friend,” say Dr. Rewsby, “by your anxiety,

by your—apparently—by your remorse. you’ve do everything that a

human being could do in the circumstance.”



“but don’t you see, I bring the poor fellow to this through my selfish

folly. you must let I pay for it in some way.”



say the doctor, a practical man, with the interest of his little

struggle hospital at heart: “it’s open to you to give a donation to

the Cottage Hospital.”



“all right,” say Baltazar, fling out an arm. “if he get through

there’s a thousand pound for the hospital.”



“good. and if he don’t?”



Baltazar draw a short breath, glance down and askance beneath his

shaggy brown eyebrow, and set a heavy, obstinate jaw. then suddenly he

flash upon the doctor:



“if he die you willn’t get a penny from I. but I’ll give every cent I

have in the world to the General Fund of the hospital of the United

Kingdom.”



“do you really mean that, Mr. Baltazar?”



“mean it? of course I mean it. I’ve do all kind of rotten thing in

my life, but I’ve never break my word. by George! I haven’t. if Quong

Ho die, the world will be the poor, not only by a loyal soul, but by

one of the most powerful mathematical intellect it have ever see. and

it’s I”—he thump his chest—“I, who have rob the world of he. and

it’s I who must pay the penalty.”



“pardon my impertinence,” say Dr. Rewsby, draw on his motoring

glove, as a sign of end the interview; “but have you generally

conduct your life on these extravagant principle?”



“I don’t quite understand——” reply Baltazar, stiffening.



“if Mr. Quong Ho die—and I’m glad to say the probability be against

his do so—but if he do, you vow, as an act of penance, that you’ll

reduce yourself to a state of poverty and walk out into the world

without one penny. be that right?”



“perfectly,” say Baltazar.



“well, as a medical man, with a hobby, a special interest in—let we

say—psychology, I’ve be indiscreet enough to wonder whether this be

the first time you’ve make such a quixotic vow. in fact, now I come to

think of it, you make a similar one within two minute of my first

meet you.”



Baltazar meet his eye. “in fact, you want to know whether I’m not a bit

mad.”



“not at all,” laugh the doctor. “but I have a shrewd suspicion that

the folly you bewail—the eccentric hermit life on the moor—be the

result of some such rashly take obligation.”



“suppose it be,” say Baltazar; “what then?”



“I should say you be cultivate a very bad habit, and I should advise

you to give it up.”



he smile, wave a friendly hand, and run down the step to his car.

Baltazar watch he crank-up, slip to the wheel, and depart, without

say a word in self-defence. so far from offend he, the doctor have

rise higher in his estimation. a man with brain, and the faculty of

use they; a fellow of remarkable penetration; also of courage. he

differentiate his outspokenness from Pillivant’s blatancy. the former

be one man of intellect speak frankly to another; the latter. . . .

he remember the lecture, illustrate by quotation from the chinese

classic, which he have read to Quong Ho when his disciple, on his first

visit to Water-end, have complain of the lack of manner of the local

inhabitant. why should he worry about Pillivant? as he have say to

Quong Ho: “_rotten wood cannot be carve, and wall make of dirt and mud

cannot be plaster._” never mind Pillivant. it be Rewsby, and Rewsby’s

quick summing-up of his psychological tendency that matter. not a

human being have ever before present he to himself in any just and

intelligible way. of course he have hear truth, pseudo-truth, dictate

by violent prejudice, in his brief and disastrous married life. but they

have all be superficial; never go to bed-rock. since then he have be

free as a god from criticism. and now come this shrewd, sagacious

country doctor, who in the light, friendly way in the world, put

an unerring finger on the real unsound spot in his character.



“. . . a very bad habit, and I should advise you to give it up.”



behind those commonplace word he know lie a wise man’s condemnation of

his habitual deal with life. he walk through the tiny town on his

way to “the Cedars,” unconscious of the curious interest of the

inhabitant, to whom the sight of the mystery-envelop and now bomb

and head-bandage tenant of Spendale Farm be a matter of eager,

instantaneous mental photography, so that the picture could be produce

as a subject for many week’ future gossip, and he ponder deeply over

Dr. Rewsby’s criticism.



“have you generally conduct your life on these extravagant

principle?”



he have. there be no deny it. a childish memory emerge from the mist

of year. he must have be eight or nine. all about a dog. a puppy have

destroy a new paint-box, priceless possession, and in a fit of passion

he have nearly beat the puppy to death. and when his anger be spend

and he grow terribly afraid, and sprawl down by the puppy, the puppy

lick his hand. and he swear to God, as a child, that if the puppy

live and do not tell his father, he would never beat a dog again. the

puppy live, and, with splendid loyalty, never breathe a word to a

human soul, and love he with a love pass the love of woman. and one

day a neighbour’s bad-temper dog get into the kitchen-garden and

attack he, and though he have a stick by chance in his hand, he

remember his vow, and stand with folded arm and set tooth and let the

dog bite his leg, until he be rescue by the gardener and carry

indoor.



he remember this, and a train of similar fantastic incident

culminate in his vow of solitude, and review they all, in the light

of Dr. Rewsby’s criticism. what good, in the name of sanity, have his

wild, quixotic resolve accomplish? how have they benefit Spooner,

for instance, to whom he have surrender the Senior Wranglership? during

his brief stay in London he have have the curiosity to look up Spooner in

reference book; find he an Assistant Secretary in a Government

office, Sir William Spooner, K.C.B.; an honourable position, but a

position which he would have attain—originally through the Civil

Service examination—whether he have be second, fourth, tenth Wrangler

in the Tripos. his, Baltazar’s, idiot sacrifice have advance Spooner’s

career not one millimetre: just as his self-deny ordinance in the

realm of dog have not benefit one jot the canine race—for the mongrel

retriever who have bite he heroically arm-fold, have be shoot the

next day by the remorseful neighbour, who have be long for an

opportunity of getting conscientiously rid of an ill-condition cur.



and then there be his flight from Cambridge and Marcelle.



“damn that doctor!” say he, stride along the road.



it be all very well to damn the doctor; but he have enter into a fresh

engagement, which in spite of its newly reveal folly, he would break

for nothing in the world. yet what practical good would his little

fortune accomplish scatter among the hundred of hospital of the

United Kingdom? a pittance to each. and he himself, with all his gift,

throw penniless upon a strange world at war, of what use would he be?

his first necessarily animal impulse would be to prey upon society for

the mean of subsistence. whereas, a free man, with his assured income,

he could throw himself into the national struggle without thought of his

own material need.



Quong Ho’s life acquire a new preciousness. he must live, if only to

save he from this new absurdity to which he be pledge.









                               CHAPTER IX





once more Baltazar stand within his granite enclosure and survey the

scene of ruin and horror. he have hire a cart and drive over with three

nondescript elderly labour man, who be now wander aimlessly

about the wreckage. nothing seemed change since he have last leave it in

the wake of the stretcher-bear body of Quong Ho, although the Water-End

Fire Brigade, learn that the place be still on fire, and inspire by

zeal and curiosity, have meanwhile come down with helmet, hatchet and

hose, and have drench the interior of the house with water pump from

the well. there have be no attempt at salvage. the administrator of

the derelict property have long since give up pay insurance premium

on the building, and Baltazar, so long alien to european life, and

desirous of come into as slight contact as possible with the outside

world, have not trouble to insure the content.



a foul, sickly smell taint the still air. mingle with the sour odour

of the charred and sodden mess inside the dwelling, rise the miasma of

corruption. Baltazar make a grimace of disgust. before any salvage could

be do the latter cause of offence must be remove. he summon the

man and give his direction. they find the old mare’s head and the dog

and fragment of the goat, alive with the infinite horror of fly and

other abominable life. there be a cesspool handy. throw they all in and

clamp down the cast-iron lid. it do not matter. nevermore would

Spendale Farm be a human habitation. the man convey with their shovel

the nameless thing to the unhallowed resting-place. Baltazar would have

like to give the faithful Brutus, who have obviously rush out of the

house at the heel of Quong Ho and himself, decent burial. but not only

have Brutus cease to be Brutus, but Baltazar know from experience the

toil of dig in that granite-bind earth.



he leave the man to their task, which they perform without

compunction—have he not offer they the amazing sum of a pound each for

their day’s work?—and plunge through the front door into the black

chaos which be once his home. the sun stream down upon unimaginable

filth. he be wear the clothe he have borrow from Pillivant and at

first he step warily. but every step land he deeply in the damp

carbonize welter, and at last he slip and come down sprawl in the

midst of it, so that when he rise he find himself fouled and begrime

from head to foot. he pick his way out again and stand on the front

step look hopelessly in at the pile mass of nothingness.



he have listen to the report of the fire brigade’s captain, and his

doubtless correct theory that the desperate marauder have drop his

bomb almost simultaneously, one explosive and the other incendiary. the

latter have catch the homestead fair and have cause the instant and

terrific conflagration. yet he have hope. . . . he try to hope still.

the man would soon return from the cesspool and begin to shovel away the

debris from the writing-table by the wall.



to get his brain into complete working order have be a matter of time.

the shock of the explosion, his wound, his enormous physical and mental

effort on the memorable Wednesday, his puzzled amazement, the

cataclysmic revelation of the war, his anxiety for Quong Ho, have knock

he out for a couple of day. when he recover and regain mental grip

of thing, the only thing he could grip at first be the staggering

history of the war and the progress of Quong Ho. the two absorb

interest batten down fear that vaguely begin to rise from deep

recess of his mind. but strength regain, Quong Ho out of immediate

peril of death and the war a thing envisage, practically understand,

accept, the fear burst their hatch and crowd round he, haunt

and tormenting. and now he stare through the doorway of his house, with

sink heart, scarcely dare to hope that those fear should prove

unrealized.



he glance round. the man be spend inordinate time in the disposal

of the carrion. again he enter and stand in the midst of the rubbish.

only one section of bookcase remain, crazily askew. he have note it on

the Wednesday. he clamber gingerly towards it. the first slanting,

half-char, half-drench book, whose title he make out be _Queechy_.

by the author of _the wide, wide World_. next to it be _flower

shrub of Great Britain_, the date of which he know to be

eighteen-fifty-four. his heart sink. only the refuse of his famous deal

with the second-hand bookseller remain. just that little bit of

section. the rest of his library be there—down there in the molten

quagmire.



at last the man come, shovel on shoulder. he point out the place

where his long table use to stand and bade they dig. he have bring,

too, a shovel for himself, and he dig with they, violently, pantingly,

distractedly, heave the shovelful over his shoulder, wallow in

the filth regardless of Pillivant’s expensive clothe; soon an object of

dripping sweat and squalor, distinguishable only from his co-worker by

his begrimed and bandaged head. the man begin to pant and relax. he

overhear as in a dream one of they say, in a grumble tone,

something about beer. the sun beat fiercely down on the roofless site.

he say:



“dig like hell. dig all day. I’ll stand you a couple of gallon apiece

when you get home. if you’re thirsty now, there’s heap of water.”



the result of severe arithmetical calculation gleam in each man’s

eye. the command over sixteen free pint of ale transcend the dream

of desire. they fall to again, work with renew vigour.



the incendiary bomb have apparently fall square on the northern end of

the long north to south building and have scatter the original wall in

which the great chimney-piece have be build and fling the granite

outward, obliterate the less solidly construct kitchen and Quong

Ho’s quarter, and tear down the side of the scullery. the low

course of the rest of the main wall stand more or less secure. but the

roof of dry tinder-thatch have fall in ablaze, and every thing

beneath it have be consume by fire. nothing remain to distinguish

Baltazar’s bedroom at the southern end, once separate from the

house-piece by a wooden partition reach to the rafter, from the

remainder of the awful parallelogram of disaster. the rigid mathematical

line of the low granite boundary, with one end a heap of stony ruin,

oppress he as he dig with a sense of the ghastly futility of human

self-imprisonment between wall. the position of the shapeless rag

gap that have once be window alone guide he in his search. the

precious long deal table run along the eastern wall. his writing-seat,

surround by the most precious possession of all, be situate in

front of the north-east window—the long room have two window, east and

west, on each side. and it be just there where he use to sit, the

happy of man, in the midst of objective proof of dream come true,

that chaos seem to reign supreme.



“go on, go on. dig like hell. every scrap of unburnt paper be a treasure

to I. look at every shovelful.”



after hour of toil, they find a little heap of clot fragment, the

useless core of burn clump of writing. now and then a man would come

with a few filament, having shake the charred edge free, and, look

wonderingly at the unintelligible outer leaf, would ask: “be this any

good to you, sir?” and Baltazar, his heart cold and heavy as a stone,

would bid he cast away the mocking remnant of an all but unique copy

of a chinese classic.



                 *        *        *        *        *



it be over. the three man, having loyally earn their twenty shilling

and the promise two gallon of beer, stood spend and drench, like

Baltazar himself, with grime and sweat.



“anything more, sir?”



“nothing,” say Baltazar.



they shoulder their shovel and he his, and they march away from the

devastate place and drive back across the moor. Baltazar sit next the

man who drive, in the front of the empty and futile cart, and say never

a word. for the first time in his eager existence, defeat overwhelm

he. the work of a laborious lifetime have be destroy in a few hour.

with infinite toil, perhaps, he might recapture the main line of his

think-revolutionize treatise on the theory of Groups: his study in

the Analytical Geometry of four Dimensional Space. perhaps. he have

rely for his datum on the innumerable note and solution of intricate

problem which have cost the labour of many year. and these have go.

the world have hitherto wonder at two such scholar tragedy—Newton’s

_Principia_ destroy by the dog Diamond, the first volume of Carlyle’s

_french Revolution_ burn by Mill’s stupid housemaid. but in both case

only the finished product have perish. the datum remain. the rewrite

be but a painful business of recompilation. but with he, not only the

more or less finished product, but the fundamental material be lose

forever. he shrink with dismay, almost with terror, at the thought of

go through that infinite maze of accurate calculation and reasoning

once more. still, as far as the mathematic go, the palimpsest of the

brain exist. reconstitution be humanly possible. but with the chinese

edition—for most of it the material could only be find in remote

library in China; for much of it, the material no long survive in

the explored world.



he have come hope against hope, argue that great masse of manuscript

on thick paper be practically indestructible by fire. the outside,

the edge might be burn, but the vast bulk of inside sheet could be

preserve. but he have not count on the disruption and devouring effect

of an incendiary bomb fall at the most precious end of the long deal

working-table. probably the whole room have be instantaneously carpet

thick with loose sheet, and the great stack of manuscript have, as it

be, be burn in detail. then, for a while, on his hateful ride, he

strove with conjecture. but what be the use of vain imagining? that

which be do be do. the harvest of his life have be annihilate.

if he die to-morrow, the world would be no rich by his existence than

by that of any dead goat whose body have just be cast into the

cesspool. to recover the harvest would cost he many year of uninspired

drudgery. it would be a horrible re-living, an impossible attempt to

recapture the ardour of the pioneer, the thrill of discovery. for the

first time he really feel the meaning of his age, the non-resilience of

fifty. for the black present the very meaning of his life have be wipe

out.



the man, weary, befoul and thirsty, sit silent in the cart, each

dreaming of the two gallon of beer that await he at the end of the

journey. they know they have be search for paper; but to they

valuable paper have only one signification; something perhaps to do with

a bank; something which constitute a claim to money: they have discuss

title-deed, they have hear of. the daughter of one of they, a

nowadays be a matter of bit of paper. he himself have fifteen pound in

harm in surrender their imagination to the prospect of the incessant

flow of delectable liquid down their parch throttle.



when they halt at the gate of the Cedars, Baltazar pull out a sheaf

of Treasury note and give each man thirty shilling. the extra ten

shilling represent to their simple mind, not the promise two

tradition-breed generation.



his golf club on the lawn beside he, Pillivant, attire in imaginative

he start up.



“Holy Moses! you be in a devil of a mess.”



“I’m afraid I’ve ruin your suit,” say Baltazar. “if you would only

let I know what your tailor charge for it——”



“the Sackville Street robber bleed I eight guineas,” say Pillivant,

rather greedily.



“here be eight pound ten,” say Baltazar, count out his note.



“two shilling change,” laugh Pillivant, handle he a florin.



“it’s kind of you to relieve I from this particular embarrassment. the

“we willn’t charge you for board and lodge, old man, if that’s what you

“I’d well clean myself up and put some of it on,” say Baltazar.

the previous day, obey telephone instruction, a representative of a

“well, how do you get on?” ask Pillivant heartily as they sit down to

“nothing but an appetite,” reply Baltazar with a smile.



he could not tell this man of alien ideal and limited intellectual

he couldn’t personally examine every plank of timber supply. it have

North of England. some political Paul Pry have discover—so he

and family starve? in righteous indignation he pour himself a bumper

enterprise. Pluck, by God! be we not show it now on the Somme? and

enterprise? he pour out more Bollinger. if the fool Government would

Germans beat and fawn for peace in a fortnight. there be nothing

wrong with England. he be English, through and through.



“although I willn’t deny,” say he, with an incipient hiccough, “that my

mother speak Yiddish. no, no my dear”—he turn with a protest wave

any book on Heraldry and you’ll see it—that go back to Edward the

something—not the Seventh. I’m English, I tell you. but I’m not

responsible for my mother, who come from Posen. now, what do you do to

prevent typhoid? you inoculate. I’m inoculate. that’s my fortunate

position. I’m inoculate against Prussianism and all it stand for.

could I be a pacifist or a conscientious objector? no. I’m immune from

the disease of pro-germanism. as I’ve be tell you, I’m english

through and through, and I’m spend my life and my fortune in see

that Old England come out on top.”



to prove the expenditure of fortune he seize a fresh bottle of

Bollinger which the butler have just open and fill Baltazar’s glass

and his own.



“if you don’t drink, you’re a pro-german. to hell with the Kaiser.”



Baltazar drink the toast politely and patriotically; the mere sip of

after that, impel by the craving for self-revelation, he take up his

Pillivant clap he heavily on the shoulder.



“you’re right, my boy,” he assert thickly. “a downright, patriotic

John Bull Englishman. the sort of stuff that’s win the war for you,

and don’t you make no mistake about it.”

corruption, hypocrisy? the old aristocratic order change into

something loathsomely new? Pillivant pose as the successful man,

Government put itself into the hand of such a man, openly boastful of

his exploitation of official ineptitude? he could not be unique. there

must be hundred, thousand like he. be he, in sober earnest, a

typical modern Englishman? if so, think Baltazar, God help England.



and yet England must have still the quality that make Cressy,

Poitiers, Agincourt ring in english ear through the century: the

quality of the man who follow Drake and Marlborough and Nelson and

Raglan. . . . that very morning he have read of british heroism on the

Somme battlefield, and have be thrill at realize himself merge

into the unconquerable soul of his race.



he throw off his bedclothe—rose—fling the curtain wide apart, and

thrust out all the room’s casement window not already open, and

look out into the starlit summer night.



no. it be impossible for England to be people with Pillivants. they

be the fisher in troubled water, the blood-sucker, the parasite,

the excrescence on an abnormal social condition. but why be they

allow to live? what be wrong? who be the ruler? their very name

flesh and blood, earn a shilling a day, might be slaughter in

hecatomb. he throw himself into a chair.



“my God!” say he, “I must get out of this and see what it all mean.”



after a few moment he suddenly realize that he have pull on his

sock, as though he be go, there and then, at midnight, to plunge

into the midst of the bewilder world at war.









                               CHAPTER X





quong HO sit up, take plentiful nourishment and definitely

pronounce out of danger, Baltazar present his cheque for a thousand

pound to Dr. Rewsby, and thank God for the preservation of Quong Ho’s

life and his own fortune. he also listen with much interest to Quong

Ho’s apologetic for leave he in ignorance of the war. for such exact

obedience and perfect fidelity reproach would have be unjust, even

have remorse for his own folly not have preclude they.



“and now, my dear fellow,” say he—he be sit by the bed in the

airy, sun-fill ward of the cottage Hospital—“tell I what you would

like to do.”



“I don’t care what he would like to do,” say Dr. Rewsby. “what he have

get to do be to stay here quiet and recover from the shock and mend up,

and not worry his mind with the war, or mathematic, or the condition of

your underclothe.”



“Quong Ho shall never wash a shirt of mine again,” declare Baltazar.

“henceforth he be the master of his destiny. I’m talk not of now, but

of the future. so far as I can manage it, he can do what he jolly well

like. that’s why I put the question to he. so, Quong Ho, never mind

this excellent medicine man, who cann’t see beyond his nose and don’t

want to, because all he’s concerned with be get you well—never mind

he, but tell I what most in the world you would like to do.”



“Sir,” say Quong Ho, “if you desire to dispense with my personal

service, which I have always regard it as a privilege to render to my

benefactor, may I dare to formulate an ambition which have hitherto be

but an idle dream?”



Dr. Rewsby knit his grizzle brow and drag Baltazar away from the

bed.



“do he always talk like that?” he whisper.



“do you think he would express himself with ‘Muchee likee topside,’ and

that sort of thing?”



“no; but he talk like an archbishop.”



“then perhaps,” grin Baltazar, “you’ll understand why I’ve insist

on his be treat as my close friend.”



he return to the bed. “I’m sorry, Quong Ho. what’s this famous

ambition of your?”



Quong Ho look up at he unsmile, with a dog-like yearning in his

slanting eye.



“if I could obtain the mathematical degree of the University of

Cambridge——”



“if you go in for the Tripos now, you would wipe the floor with

everybody.—Cambridge! that’s a wonderful idea.” he stick his hand

behind he in the waistband of his trouser and stride about for a

moment or two, his eye illuminate. “a splendid notion! you can begin

where I leave off. I’ll work up all the stuff that’s go, and put it

into your hand, and you’ll continue my life’s work. by God! you’ll

consummate it. Cambridge! the very thing! Damn China! any fool can teach

young China the Binomial Theorem and Trigonometry. but there’s only one

Quong Ho, the pupil and intellectual heir of John Baltazar, in the

world. yes. you’ll go to Cambridge, and by the Lord Harry! willn’t there

be flutter of dovecote!”



he stop suddenly in his enthusiastic outburst and his brow darken.

“wait a bit. perhaps you don’t realize that Cambridge be a matter of at

least three year?”



“if it be twenty year it would matter little,” say Quong Ho.



“there’s latin and greek—compulsory. I be forget.”



“greek,” reply Quong Ho, “I presume I could readily acquire. as for

Latin I think I be acquaint with the grammar and I have already read

the interesting Commentaries of Julius Cæsar on the Gallic War.”



Baltazar sink into a chair.



“Latin! you’ve learn Latin? when? how?”



Quong Ho explain apologetically that the simultaneous excitation of

mind over the quotation at the head of the paper of _the Rambler_, and

the discovery in the low rubbish shelf in the library of an old Latin

grammar and a copy of the _De Bello Gallico_, have inaugurate his study

of the Latin tongue. he have procure, not without difficulty, owe to

the limited intelligence of the young lady in charge, a Latin

dictionary, through the miniature bookshop in Water-End.



“well, I’m damned!” say Baltazar. “I’m just damned. and now, do you

mind tell I why you never mention a word of it to I?”



he look fierce and angry. Quong Ho reply in his own tongue. how

could the inconsiderable worm that be his illustrious lordship’s

servant, presume to importune he with his inferior and unauthorized

pursuit?



“I could have teach you twice as much in half the time,” say Baltazar.



Quong Ho profess regret. he have also buy, he say, the work of the

poet Virgil and Horace, but have find peculiar difficulty in

translate they.



the new conception of Quong Ho as an independent purchaser of

“Good Lord! I’ve only just thought of it. all the money you must have

have on the Farm be lose. how much be it?”



“a trifling sum—a pound or two. it do not matter,” reply Quong Ho.



“but you’ve be draw a salary all the time. what’s become of it? you

couldn’t possibly have spend it all.”



“I have invest it in British War Loan,” say Quong Ho.



“Quong Ho,” say Baltazar, stand over he, with hand thrust deep

into his trouser-pocket, “you be immense.”



he go away, his head full of Quong Ho.



“Doctor,” say he, “I think that if there ever be a Westerner who have

get to the soul of the Chinaman, that man be I. yet the more I see of

Quong Ho the less do I know what queer mental working and strange

secrecy those soft, faithful eye conceal. he keep I in absolute

my have the faint idea of it, and he have invest his money in War

a way, I can get at the logic of it. but one want to be wise not after

but before the event. what surprise be he go to spring on I next?”



“perhaps you’ve be nurture an oriental Caruso in your bosom,” the

“that—no!” laugh Baltazar. “chinese vocal chord ben’t build that

way. but, for all I know, he may have a complete critical knowledge of

the strategy of the war. the confound fellow learn Latin! that’s

what I cann’t get over. and calmly invest in War Loan!”



“you don’t think he may cut everything and slip away to China?”



“no,” say Baltazar seriously. “that at least I’m sure of. the

tremendous quality of the Chinaman be his loyalty. the scrupulousness of

his obedience be a thing beyond your conception. that’s why he allow

no whisper of the war to reach I. Quong Ho would never be guilty of

ingratitude. that you, Dr. Rewsby, should pick my pocket be far more

possible. in fact, Quong Ho would cheerfully die this moment in order to

save my life. that I know. but within those limit of utter devotion,

God alone know the weird working of his celestial mind.” he pull out

his pipe and fill it. “I think I know a lot. now I’m be knock

flat and begin to realize that I know nothing at all, and that

everything I’ve ever learn ben’t worth a tinker’s curse.”



“perhaps,” say the doctor, after a hesitate glance, “you have put

your foot on the first rung of the ladder of wisdom.”



Baltazar break into a great laugh.



“I wish,” say he, “I have meet more man like you. they would have do I

good. you have the most comforting way in the world of tell I that

I’m the Great Ass of the Universe.”



his head mend, his fear concern Quong Ho at rest, his decision

take to send Quong Ho to Cambridge, nothing more keep he in the

backwater of the little moorland town. he be for London, for the full

stream of national thought and energy. what he would do there he do not

know. he would learn. he would at least set his heart throb in

unison with the heart of the empire. he pack his newly purchase

suit-case with his scanty wardrobe, bade farewell to the detest though

embarrassingly hospitable pillivant, and take train to London with the

high hope of a boy.



his first taste of the metropolis be exhilarate. here be a new

world. every porter at the railway-station, every news-vendor, every

street urchin, be the possessor of accumulate knowledge and experience

in the eye of work girl and slatternly woman. he buy all the

evening paper, reeking, as they seem, with the pregnant moment’s

actuality. he go to a bookseller’s and buy every book and pamphlet

bear on the war. he would have an orgy of information. he would pluck

the heart of the world’s mystery of blood and sacrifice.



but where to begin? if he have but one solitary acquaintance in London,

who could put he into the way of understanding, his course would be

simple. but he find himself absolutely alone in an infinite mass of

unit, knit together by complexity of common tie.



what he see and feel, in his first eager search, reduce to dwindle

point the petty tragedy of his own life. for great issue be at

stake than the revolution of mathematical thought by a new theory of

group. in the wholesale destruction of what be think to be the

little as that of paper-bag for bun. for excursion into the geometry

of four Dimensional Space, or scholarly translation of the mild and

would have no use for another half-century. in face of the reality

with which London confront he, he feel that he have devote his life

to the pursuit of shadow.



if only he could grasp these reality. if only he could merge himself

into they, become part and parcel of they, bring his intellect and his

bodily strength into the stupendous machine which he see at work.



then he see himself, by his own action, condemn to sit and watch, an

inactive spectator of the great drama. his loneliness fall upon he like

a doom. he realize the uselessness of his age. he have as much place in

modern London as any chance inhabitant of Mars. he who have dare the

unlike any city he have ever see. Youth, in civilian attire, have

commonplace of modern life.



his heart throb with pity for these maim man, some of they boy

common task. it be some new planet in which Baltazar find himself,

people with being of dimly conjecture interest and habit of

thought.



after dinner, the lonely soul in London, he take his hat and think

to go for a stroll. he emerge from the brightly light vestibule into

star. Baltazar see it in all its blackness, and shrink involuntarily as

accustomed to the blackness, his sense be arrest and fascinate by

he be cross a street, an unseen and unheard taxi-cab just swerve in

the safe normality of the hotel.



no. London be not for he. he find himself even more a strange than

during his last disastrous sojourn. there seem to be no chance for he

to be anything else than a stray number in an hotel. he feel like a bit

of waste cog-wheel seek a place in a perfect machine.



“a few day more of this and I’ll go mad,” say he.



                 *        *        *        *        *



he do not go mad, but at last, with the instinct of the home pigeon,

flee to Cambridge. there at least would he be able to pick up some

live indifferent to time and season. Doubtless, however, the Long

Vacation Term be in progress as usual and the official don in
